أن يكون مسلكاً من مسالك العلة لأن الوصف الذي ينفيه السبر ، إما أن يكون ظاهر المناسبة أي مشتملاً على مصلحة فإن اشتمل على مصلحة . فإما أن تكون منضبطة الفهم فهو المناسبة . وإما كلية لا تنضبط أي لا يقطع بوجودها أو عدمها فهو الشبه ، وإما لا يكون مشتملاً على مصلحة فهو الطردي . فلا بد في العلة من اعتبار وجود المصلحة أو صلاحيتها ولا يمكن أن يكون هذا إلا بمناسبة أو طرد أو شبه . فالدليل على التعديل هو واحد من هؤلاء ، أما السبر فهو شرط لا دليل . لكي يتخلص الزركشي من هذا النزاع بين القائلين بأن السبر والتقسيم هل هو دليل أو شرط دليل ، قرر أن هذا المسلك عام . أي أنه يدخل في جميع المسالك فهو شرط دليل إذن . ثم هو مسلك بذاته . أي أنه دليل. ومن الواضح أن السبر والتقسيم عنصر منطقي . وقد حاول الأصوليون المتأخرون أنفسهم رده إلى القياس الشرطي المنفصل أي رده إلى قياس استثنائي كبراه شرطية منفصلة حقيقية أو مانعة جمع ـ وصغراه رفع أحد المتنافيين ، فينتج إثبات آخر . ولكن هل هناك صلة بين الإثنين ؟ من المؤكد أنه لم توجد أدنى صلة في العصور الأولى ، أي إلى عصر الغزالي . بحيث لا نجد أحداً من الأصوليين المتقدمين حاول رده الى المنطق اليوناني ، لكن نرى هذه المحاولة لدى المتأخرين من الأصوليين . وقد اصطلح متأخرو الأصوليين على رد طرق البحث الأصولية إلى المنطق اليوناني . والغاية من هذه المحاولة هي القضاء على أي اعتراض يوجه إلى حركة مزج منطق اليونان وأرسطو على الخصوص بعلوم المسلمين ، بدعوى أن متقدمي الأصوليين والمتكلمين لجأوا هم أنفسهم إلى ما يشبه المنطق ، أو ما يمكن رده إلى المنطق اليوناني . ولكننا سنرى فيما بعد أن ابن تيمية يقرر أن المسلمين توصلوا بأنفسهم وبدون معرفة للمنطق الأرسططاليسي إلى كثير من صور هذا المنطق ولكن في أسلوبهم الخاص وفي عباراتهم الخاصة . وعلى فرض أن المسلمين عرفوا فكرة السبر والتقسيم من منطق اليونان . فينبغي أن نلاحظ أنهم لم يستمدوها من منطق أرسطو بل من منطق الرواقيين فأرسطو لم يعرف ـ كما بينا من قبل الأقيسة الشرطية متصلة كانت أو منفصلة . غير أني أستطيع أن أجزم بأن المسلمين لم يأخذوا فكرة السبر والتقسيم عن المنطق اليوناني . وسنرى كثيرين من المناطقة المتأخرين يعتبرون السبر والتقسيم ـ إذا ما اعتبرناه طريقاً قائماً بذاته لا مسلكاً للعلة ـ ضعيفاً ولا يفيد اليقين . وهذا ما يجعله مخالفاً لطبيعة القياس حملياً كان أو شرطياً ـ فإن هذا القياس يؤدي إلى اليقين.
That it is a method from among the methods of the cause, because the description that is negated by examination is either apparent in suitability, i.e., containing a benefit. If it contains a benefit, then either it is precisely understood, so it is suitability; or it is general and not precise, i.e., one cannot ascertain its existence or non-existence, so it is similarity; or it does not contain a benefit, so it is coincidental. So in the cause, consideration of the existence of benefit or its suitability is necessary, and this cannot be except through suitability, coincidence, or similarity. So the proof of transitivity is one of these; as for examination, it is a condition, not a proof. In order for al-Zarkashi to extricate himself from this dispute between those who say whether examination and division is a proof or a condition of a proof, he decided that this method is general, i.e., it enters into all methods, so it is a condition of a proof then; then it is a method in itself, i.e., it is a proof. And it is clear that examination and division is a logical element. Later legal theorists themselves attempted to reduce it to the disjunctive conditional syllogism, i.e., reduce it to an exceptional syllogism whose major premise is a true disjunctive conditional or a preventer of conjunction, and its minor premise is the removal of one of the two mutually exclusive things, resulting in the affirmation of the other. But is there a connection between the two? It is certain that there was no connection whatsoever in the early centuries, i.e., up to the age of al-Ghazali. Such that we do not find any of the early legal theorists attempting to reduce it to Greek logic, but we see this attempt among later legal theorists. And later legal theorists agreed to reduce the methods of legal research to Greek logic. The aim of this attempt is to eliminate any objection directed at the movement of mixing the logic of the Greeks and especially Aristotle with the sciences of the Muslims, under the claim that the early legal theorists and theologians resorted themselves to something resembling logic, or something that can be reduced to Greek logic. But we will see later that Ibn Taymiyya affirms that the Muslims arrived by themselves, without knowledge of Aristotelian logic, at many forms of this logic, but in their own style and in their own expressions. And assuming that the Muslims knew the idea of examination and division from the logic of the Greeks, then we should note that they did not derive it from Aristotle's logic but from the logic of the Stoics; for Aristotle did not know, as we showed before, conditional syllogisms, whether conjunctive or disjunctive. However, I can assert that the Muslims did not take the idea of examination and division from Greek logic. And we will see many later logicians consider examination and division—if we consider it an independent path, not a method for the cause—as weak and not yielding certainty. And this makes it contrary to the nature of syllogism, categorical or conditional—for this syllogism yields certainty.

Al-Tard: Definition, Distinction from Dawaran, and Critique
Author defines al-tard and distinguishes it from dawaran

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish al-tard from al-dawaran and evaluate the strength of a tard-based argument in usul al-fiqh.

Core Idea
Tard considers existence only; dawaran considers both existence and non-existence.

In the search for the effective cause ('illa) in qiyas, legal theorists developed methods to test candidate descriptions. One such method is al-tard (coincidence), where the description accompanies the ruling whenever the ruling exists. But is this enough to prove causation? This passage defines tard, contrasts it with the stronger dawaran, and notes a serious critique: the Companions never relied on it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of tard
Locate the precise definition: tard is the coincidence of a description with the ruling in existence only, not in non-existence. Note that the description must be neither suitable (munasib) nor similar (shabih).
This is a negative condition: the description lacks any apparent suitability or similarity.
2
Understand the scope of tard
The description must coincide with the ruling in all cases except the disputed branch (the case under judgment). Some scholars restrict tard to a single case, but the author argues that would turn it into dawaran.
Consistency across multiple cases strengthens the claim of causality.
3
Distinguish tard from dawaran
Dawaran requires coincidence in both existence and non-existence: when the description exists, the ruling exists; when the description is absent, the ruling is absent. Tard only requires coincidence in existence.
Dawaran is considered a stronger indicator because it includes negative correlation.
4
Examine the critique of tard
The author argues that tard is difficult to verify and was never used by the Companions. They never based a ruling on a description that neither suits the ruling nor arouses likeness.
This critique undermines the methodological value of tard.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ب ـ المسلك الثاني الطرد : الطرد هو مقارنة الوصف للحكم في الوجود دون العدم، بحيث لا يكون مناسباً ولا شبيهاً . على أن تكون هذه المقارنة في جميع الصور ما عدا الصورة المتنازع فيها . أي صورة الفرع الذي يراد ثبوت الحكم له لوجود ذلك الوصف فيه، بناء على أن ذلك الوصف الطردي علة لهذا الحكم . على أن بعض الأصوليين يرى أن الوصف الطردي يقارن الحكم في صورة واحدة لا في جميع صوره . وفي الواقع إن هذا الوصف الأخير يخرجه عن أن يكون طرداً إلى أن يكون دوراناً . وفسره البعض الآخر بأنه حمل الفرع على الأصل بغير أوصاف الأصل من غير أن يكون لذلك الوصف تأثير في إثبات الحكم . ويخلط بعض الأصوليين بين الدوران والطرد . والفرق بينهما أن الدوران مقارنة الوصف للحكم وجوداً وعدماً، والطرد مقارنته وجوداً فقط . ويؤخذ على هذا المسلك أنه صعب التحقيق ولم يلجأ اليه أصوليو الصحابة، فلم يثبت عنهم قط الحكم بطرد لا يناسب الحكم ولا يثير شبهاً. أن الإطراد عبارة عن مقارنة الوصف للعلة وجوداً، بحيث لا يوجد الوصف إلا ويوجد معه الحكم، ولا يمكن التوصل إلى إثبات هذا، إلا إذا اقترن الحكم
B – The second method: al-tard. Al-tard is the coincidence of the description with the ruling in existence without non-existence, such that it is neither suitable nor similar. This coincidence must be in all cases except the case under dispute, i.e., the case of the branch for which the ruling is intended to be established due to the presence of that description in it, based on the assumption that that coincidental description is a cause for this ruling. Although some legal theorists hold that the coincidental description coincides with the ruling in one case, not in all its cases. In reality, this latter description takes it out of being tard to being dawaran. And others interpreted it as attaching the branch to the original without the attributes of the original, without that description having any effect in establishing the ruling. Some legal theorists confuse dawaran and tard. The difference between them is that dawaran is the coincidence of the description with the ruling in existence and non-existence, while tard is its coincidence in existence only. This method is criticized as being difficult to verify, and the legal theorists among the Companions never resorted to it, for it has never been established from them a ruling by tard that does not suit the ruling nor arouses resemblance. Al-itrad is the coincidence of the description with the cause in existence, such that the description does not exist except that the ruling exists with it, and it is not possible to arrive at establishing this unless the ruling is accompanied by...
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage defines the second method (maslak) for identifying the 'illa known as al-tard (coincidence). The author begins by giving a precise definition: tard is the coincidence of a description (wasf) with a ruling (hukm) in terms of existence only, not in non-existence. The description is not suitable (munasib) nor similar (shabih). This coincidence must hold in all cases except the disputed case (the branch) where the ruling is sought. However, some legal theorists restrict tard to a single case, which the author says actually turns it into dawaran (correlation). Others interpret tard as simply applying the branch to the original without any attribute from the original having a real effect. The author then distinguishes tard from dawaran: dawaran requires coincidence in both existence and non-existence, while tard requires only existence. Finally, the author critiques this method: it is difficult to verify, and the Companions (sahaba) never used it because they never based a ruling on a description that neither suits the ruling nor arouses a likeness. The passage ends by defining al-itrad (consistent coincidence) as a stricter form where the description and cause accompany each other in existence.

الدوران
الإطراد
الوصف الطردي

Key Takeaway
Tard is a weak indicator of causality because it only checks positive correlation, and it was never used by the Companions.

Defining Tard and Distinguishing It from Dawaran
Defining tard and distinguishing it from dawaran.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define tard and distinguish it from dawaran based on their relation to existence and non-existence.

Core Idea
Tard is coextensiveness in existence only; dawaran requires coextensiveness in both existence and non-existence.

In Islamic legal theory, identifying the cause ('illa) of a ruling is crucial for analogical reasoning. Two key methods are tard and dawaran, often confused. This passage from the lesson clarifies their distinct definitions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Locate the definition of 'tard' in the Arabic passage. It is described as a comparison (muqarana) of an attribute with a ruling.
The term 'tard' is derived from the root meaning to repeat or be constant.
2
Extract the genus and differentia
The genus is 'comparison' and the differentia is 'in existence only, not in non-existence', and the attribute is neither appropriate nor similar.
The condition 'all cases except the disputed' is part of the definition.
3
Contrast with dawaran
Dawaran includes both existence and non-existence. The passage explicitly states: 'al-dawaran is comparison in existence and non-existence, while tard is comparison in existence only'.
This distinction is essential to avoid confusion.
4
Understand the scope of tard
Tard must cover all cases except the branch case. Some scholars restrict it to one case, but the author notes that would turn it into dawaran.
The branch is the new case where the ruling is to be established.

Source Text

ب ـ المسلك الثاني الطرد : الطرد هو مقارنة الوصف للحكم في الوجود دون العدم ، بحيث لا يكون مناسباً ولا شبيهاً . على أن تكون هذه المقارنة في جميع الصور ما عدا الصورة المتنازع فيها . أي صورة الفرع الذي يراد ثبوت الحكم له لوجود ذلك الوصف فيه ، بناء على أن ذلك الوصف الطردي علة لهذا الحكم . على أن بعض الأصوليين يرى أن الوصف الطردي يقارن الحكم في صورة واحدة لا في جميع صوره . وفي الواقع إن هذا الوصف الأخير يخرجه عن أن يكون طرداً إلى أن يكون دوراناً(١) . وفسره البعض الآخر بأنه حمل الفرع على الأصل بغير أوصاف الأصل من غير أن يكون لذلك الوصف تأثير في إثبات الحكم . ويخلط بعض الأصوليين بين الدوران والطرد . والفرق بينهما أن الدوران مقارنة الوصف للحكم وجوداً وعدماً ، والطرد مقارنته وجوداً فقط . ويؤخذ على هذا المسلك أن الإطراد عبارة عن مقارنة الوصف للعلة وجوداً ، بحيث لا يوجد الوصف إلا ويوجد معه الحكم ، ولا يمكن التوصل إلى إثبات هذا ، إلا إذا اقترن الحكم مع الوصف في الفرع . فإذا ما ثبت الحكم في الفرع ، يكون ذلك الوصف علة . وأثبتنا عليته بكونه مطرداً . لزم الدور . ويورد القرافي من شراح المحصول رداً على هذا الاعتراض ـ بأن الطرد هو الاستدلال بمقارنة الوصف للحكم في جميع الصور ما عدا صورة الفرع فلا يلزم الدور(١) . (٣) قد يحدث الإطراد بدون وجود العلة كالحد مع الحدود . والجوهر مع العرض ، وذات الله مع صفاته ، فإن كلاً منهما مقارن الآخر ولا علية بينهما . ويرد على هذا بأن غاية هذا الاعتراض هو حصول الطرد منفكاً عن العلية في بعض الصور ، وليس في هذا مطلقاً قدح في دلالته على العلية فالغيم دليل المطر ، ثم عدم نزول المطر في بعض الصور لا يقدح في كونه دليلاً . وعلاوة على ذلك فإن المسالك المختلفة من مناسبة ودوران وتأثير وإيماء ، قد تنفك عن العلية ولا يقدح في كونها دليلاً على العلية(٢) . أما الذين أخذوا بالطرد فعللوا هذا بالأمور الآتية : أولاً ـ يقولون « إننا إذا علمنا أن هذا الحكم لا بد له من علة ، وعلمنا بهذا الوصف مع خلوة الذهن عن سائر الأوصاف ، فلا شك أنه يحصل الظن بأن هذا الوصف علة لهذا الحكم لأنه لا جائز أن لا يكون لهذا الحكم علة ، ولا جائز أن تكون العلة وصفاً آخر غيره لعدم الشعور به »(٣) ويعترض على هذا بأن خلو الذهن عن سائر الأوصاف غير معقول ولا يعقل مطلقاً من إنسان يتوجه نحو الاستدلال على شيء معين « ولا يقع من مجتهد يسعى للوصول إلى ما يثير الظن » ثانياً ـ يدل الاستقراء على أن النادر يلحق بالغالب ، فإذا وصلنا إلى أن الوصف في جميع الصور المقارنة لمحل النزاع مقارن للحكم ، ثم رأينا الوصف حاصلاً في الفرع وجب أن يثبت له الحكم إلحاقاً لتلك الصورة الواحدة بسائر الصور . والمقصود هنا بالإستقراء الإستقراء الناقص . ثالثاً ـ إذا ما رأينا فرس
B. The second path: Tard. Tard is the comparison of the attribute with the ruling in existence without non-existence, such that it is neither appropriate nor similar. This comparison must be in all cases except the disputed case, i.e., the case of the branch for which the ruling is desired to be established due to the existence of that attribute in it, based on the assumption that that tardive attribute is the cause of this ruling. Some usulis hold that the tardive attribute compares with the ruling in one case only, not in all its cases. In reality, this last description removes it from being tard to being dawaran (coextensiveness in existence and non-existence). Others interpreted it as subsuming the branch under the original without the attributes of the original, without that attribute having any effect in establishing the ruling. Some usulis confuse dawaran and tard. The difference between them is that dawaran is the comparison of the attribute with the ruling in existence and non-existence, while tard is its comparison in existence only. This path is objected to: that coextensiveness (itrad) is the comparison of the attribute with the cause in existence, such that the attribute does not exist except that the ruling exists with it, and it is impossible to establish this unless the ruling accompanies the attribute in the branch. So when the ruling is established in the branch, that attribute becomes the cause, and we have proven its causality by virtue of being coextensive, then circularity (dawr) occurs. Al-Qarafi, a commentator on al-Mahsul, responds to this objection: that tard is the inference by comparing the attribute with the ruling in all cases except the branch case, so no circularity occurs. (3) Coextensiveness may occur without the existence of the cause, such as a limit with limits, substance with accident, and the essence of God with His attributes, for each is concomitant with the other with no causality between them. The response to this is that the utmost of this objection is that coextensiveness occurs disjoined from causality in some cases, and this does not at all discredit its indication of causality, for the cloud is a sign of rain, and the non-descent of rain in some cases does not discredit its being a sign. Moreover, the various paths: appropriateness, coextensiveness (in both existence and non-existence), efficacy, and suggestion, may be disjoined from causality without discrediting their being signs of causality. As for those who accepted tard, they justified it with the following: First — they say: 'If we know that this ruling must have a cause, and we know this attribute while the mind is devoid of other attributes, then there is no doubt that speculation occurs that this attribute is the cause of the ruling, because it is impossible that the ruling has no cause, and impossible that the cause be another attribute due to lack of awareness of it.' The objection to this is that the mind being devoid of other attributes is inconceivable, and cannot be absolutely conceived of a person directing himself toward inference about something specific, 'nor does it occur from a mujtahid seeking to reach what arouses speculation.' Second — Induction indicates that the rare follows the common. So if we establish that the attribute in all cases adjacent to the disputed case is concomitant with the ruling, then we see the attribute present in the branch, the ruling must be established for it, annexing that single case to the rest of the cases. What is meant here by induction is incomplete induction. Third — If we see a horse...
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage defines and critiques the logical method known as 'tard' (coextensiveness) used in Islamic jurisprudence to identify the 'illa (cause) of a legal ruling. The author begins by defining tard as the coexistence of an attribute with a ruling in cases of existence only, without considering non-existence. The attribute should not be 'appropriate' (munasib) or 'similar' to the ruling; it is simply a constant concomitant. The comparison must cover all cases except the disputed case (the branch). Some usulis restrict tard to a single case, but the author notes this would turn it into dawaran (coextensiveness in both existence and non-existence), which is different. The difference is clarified: dawaran compares the attribute with the ruling in both existence and non-existence, while tard only in existence. The author then presents a major objection to tard: if coextensiveness means that wherever the attribute is found the ruling is found, then to prove causality we must first establish that the ruling accompanies the attribute in the branch, leading to circular reasoning (dawr). Al-Qarafi's response is presented: tard infers by comparing the attribute with the ruling in all cases except the branch, so no circularity occurs. Another objection is that coextensiveness can exist without causality, as in the examples of a limit with other limits, substance with accident, or Divine essence with attributes — they coexist but are not causally related. The response is that occasional disjunction does not disprove indication, just as clouds are a sign of rain even when rain sometimes doesn't fall. Moreover, all methods of identifying cause (appropriateness, dawaran, efficacy, suggestion) can be disjoined from causality occasionally without losing their indicative value. Finally, the author presents three arguments of those who accept tard. First: if we know the ruling must have a cause and our mind is emptied of other attributes, we infer that the observed attribute is the cause; this is criticized as unrealistic for a mujtahid. Second: incomplete induction (istiqra' naqis) shows that the rare follows the common, so if the attribute accompanies the ruling in most cases, it likely does in the branch. Third: an example about a horse is introduced but cut off.

Key Terms
الطرد
الدوران
الوصف الطردي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the key difference between tard and dawaran?
A) Tard compares attribute with ruling in existence and non-existence, dawaran only in existence.
B) Tard compares attribute with ruling in existence only, dawaran in both existence and non-existence.
C) Tard involves appropriate attributes, dawaran involves similar attributes.
D) Tard uses complete induction, dawaran uses incomplete induction.

Key Takeaway
The key difference between tard and dawaran is that tard requires coextensiveness only in existence, while dawaran requires it in both existence and non-existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify whether the following is an example of tard or dawaran: The attribute 'being a liquid' always exists with the ruling 'impurity' for alcoholic beverages, but non-alcoholic liquids exist without the ruling. (Answer: tard, because non-existence is not considered.)
?
Explain in one sentence the difference between tard and dawaran using the concepts of existence and non-existence.

The Circularity Objection Against Tard and Al-Qarafi's Response
Presenting the circularity objection and al-Qarafi's response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the circularity objection against tard and how al-Qarafi refutes it.

Core Idea
The objection that tard leads to circular reasoning is avoided because tard bases its inference on cases other than the disputed one.

One of the strongest objections to tard is that it involves circular reasoning (dawr). Al-Qarafi, a leading commentator on al-Razi's al-Mahsul, provides a clear and effective response that is widely accepted in usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The objector argues: If tard means 'wherever the attribute exists, the ruling exists', then to prove that the attribute is the cause in the branch, we already need the ruling to be established in that branch. This is circular.
Circular reasoning (dawr) occurs when a premise depends on the conclusion.
2
Locate al-Qarafi's response
Al-Qarafi responds that tard does not require the ruling in the branch. It uses comparison in all other cases except the branch. Therefore, no circularity occurs.
The inference is: the attribute coexists with the ruling in all known cases, so when it appears in the branch, we infer the ruling.
3
Analytically verify the response
The objection assumes tard needs the ruling in the branch to establish coextensiveness, but al-Qarafi shows that coextensiveness is already established before considering the branch.
Thus, the branch is the test case, not the source of proof.
4
Apply to a concrete example
Example: All known wines are prohibited and intoxicating. For a new drink, we observe intoxication and infer prohibition without needing the ruling first.
This avoids the circle.

Objection: Coextensiveness Without Causality and the Cloud-Rain Analogy
Presenting the objection of coextensiveness without causality and the response using the cloud-rain analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why occasional coextensiveness without causality does not undermine tard as an indicator, using the cloud-rain analogy.

Core Idea
Coextensiveness can exist without causality, but its occasional absence does not negate its indicative value.

Another important objection against tard is that two things can be coextensive without one causing the other—like God's essence and attributes. The author responds with a powerful analogy: clouds are signs of rain even if rain sometimes does not fall. This response is key to defending probabilistic reasoning in usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The objector gives examples where coextensiveness exists without causality: a limit with other limits, substance with accident, and the essence of God with His attributes.
These are cases where two things always coexist but there is no causal relationship.
2
Understand the response
The author argues that occasional disjunction does not discredit indication. Even clouds are signs of rain despite occasional failures.
The point: an indicator does not need to be infallible to be useful.
3
Extend the analogy
The author notes that all methods of identifying cause—appropriateness, dawaran, efficacy, suggestion—can be disjoined from causality without losing their indicative value.
Thus, the objection is not specific to tard.
4
Assess the logical impact
The objection only shows exceptions, not a complete refutation. As long as tard generally indicates causality, it remains valid.
This is a common approach in usul: probabilistic methods are accepted.

Source Text

ب ـ المسلك الثاني الطرد : الطرد هو مقارنة الوصف للحكم في الوجود دون العدم ، بحيث لا يكون مناسباً ولا شبيهاً . على أن تكون هذه المقارنة في جميع الصور ما عدا الصورة المتنازع فيها . أي صورة الفرع الذي يراد ثبوت الحكم له لوجود ذلك الوصف فيه ، بناء على أن ذلك الوصف الطردي علة لهذا الحكم . على أن بعض الأصوليين يرى أن الوصف الطردي يقارن الحكم في صورة واحدة لا في جميع صوره . وفي الواقع إن هذا الوصف الأخير يخرجه عن أن يكون طرداً إلى أن يكون دوراناً(١) . وفسره البعض الآخر بأنه حمل الفرع على الأصل بغير أوصاف الأصل من غير أن يكون لذلك الوصف تأثير في إثبات الحكم . ويخلط بعض الأصوليين بين الدوران والطرد . والفرق بينهما أن الدوران مقارنة الوصف للحكم وجوداً وعدماً ، والطرد مقارنته وجوداً فقط . ويؤخذ على هذا المسلك أن الإطراد عبارة عن مقارنة الوصف للعلة وجوداً ، بحيث لا يوجد الوصف إلا ويوجد معه الحكم ، ولا يمكن التوصل إلى إثبات هذا ، إلا إذا اقترن الحكم مع الوصف في الفرع . فإذا ما ثبت الحكم في الفرع ، يكون ذلك الوصف علة . وأثبتنا عليته بكونه مطرداً . لزم الدور . ويورد القرافي من شراح المحصول رداً على هذا الاعتراض ـ بأن الطرد هو الاستدلال بمقارنة الوصف للحكم في جميع الصور ما عدا صورة الفرع فلا يلزم الدور(١) . (٣) قد يحدث الإطراد بدون وجود العلة كالحد مع الحدود . والجوهر مع العرض ، وذات الله مع صفاته ، فإن كلاً منهما مقارن الآخر ولا علية بينهما . ويرد على هذا بأن غاية هذا الاعتراض هو حصول الطرد منفكاً عن العلية في بعض الصور ، وليس في هذا مطلقاً قدح في دلالته على العلية فالغيم دليل المطر ، ثم عدم نزول المطر في بعض الصور لا يقدح في كونه دليلاً . وعلاوة على ذلك فإن المسالك المختلفة من مناسبة ودوران وتأثير وإيماء ، قد تنفك عن العلية ولا يقدح في كونها دليلاً على العلية(٢) . أما الذين أخذوا بالطرد فعللوا هذا بالأمور الآتية : أولاً ـ يقولون « إننا إذا علمنا أن هذا الحكم لا بد له من علة ، وعلمنا بهذا الوصف مع خلوة الذهن عن سائر الأوصاف ، فلا شك أنه يحصل الظن بأن هذا الوصف علة لهذا الحكم لأنه لا جائز أن لا يكون لهذا الحكم علة ، ولا جائز أن تكون العلة وصفاً آخر غيره لعدم الشعور به »(٣) ويعترض على هذا بأن خلو الذهن عن سائر الأوصاف غير معقول ولا يعقل مطلقاً من إنسان يتوجه نحو الاستدلال على شيء معين « ولا يقع من مجتهد يسعى للوصول إلى ما يثير الظن » ثانياً ـ يدل الاستقراء على أن النادر يلحق بالغالب ، فإذا وصلنا إلى أن الوصف في جميع الصور المقارنة لمحل النزاع مقارن للحكم ، ثم رأينا الوصف حاصلاً في الفرع وجب أن يثبت له الحكم إلحاقاً لتلك الصورة الواحدة بسائر الصور . والمقصود هنا بالإستقراء الإستقراء الناقص . ثالثاً ـ إذا ما رأينا فرس
B. The second path: Tard. Tard is the comparison of the attribute with the ruling in existence without non-existence, such that it is neither appropriate nor similar. This comparison must be in all cases except the disputed case, i.e., the case of the branch for which the ruling is desired to be established due to the existence of that attribute in it, based on the assumption that that tardive attribute is the cause of this ruling. Some usulis hold that the tardive attribute compares with the ruling in one case only, not in all its cases. In reality, this last description removes it from being tard to being dawaran (coextensiveness in existence and non-existence). Others interpreted it as subsuming the branch under the original without the attributes of the original, without that attribute having any effect in establishing the ruling. Some usulis confuse dawaran and tard. The difference between them is that dawaran is the comparison of the attribute with the ruling in existence and non-existence, while tard is its comparison in existence only. This path is objected to: that coextensiveness (itrad) is the comparison of the attribute with the cause in existence, such that the attribute does not exist except that the ruling exists with it, and it is impossible to establish this unless the ruling accompanies the attribute in the branch. So when the ruling is established in the branch, that attribute becomes the cause, and we have proven its causality by virtue of being coextensive, then circularity (dawr) occurs. Al-Qarafi, a commentator on al-Mahsul, responds to this objection: that tard is the inference by comparing the attribute with the ruling in all cases except the branch case, so no circularity occurs. (3) Coextensiveness may occur without the existence of the cause, such as a limit with limits, substance with accident, and the essence of God with His attributes, for each is concomitant with the other with no causality between them. The response to this is that the utmost of this objection is that coextensiveness occurs disjoined from causality in some cases, and this does not at all discredit its indication of causality, for the cloud is a sign of rain, and the non-descent of rain in some cases does not discredit its being a sign. Moreover, the various paths: appropriateness, coextensiveness (in both existence and non-existence), efficacy, and suggestion, may be disjoined from causality without discrediting their being signs of causality. As for those who accepted tard, they justified it with the following: First — they say: 'If we know that this ruling must have a cause, and we know this attribute while the mind is devoid of other attributes, then there is no doubt that speculation occurs that this attribute is the cause of the ruling, because it is impossible that the ruling has no cause, and impossible that the cause be another attribute due to lack of awareness of it.' The objection to this is that the mind being devoid of other attributes is inconceivable, and cannot be absolutely conceived of a person directing himself toward inference about something specific, 'nor does it occur from a mujtahid seeking to reach what arouses speculation.' Second — Induction indicates that the rare follows the common. So if we establish that the attribute in all cases adjacent to the disputed case is concomitant with the ruling, then we see the attribute present in the branch, the ruling must be established for it, annexing that single case to the rest of the cases. What is meant here by induction is incomplete induction. Third — If we see a horse...
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage defines and critiques the logical method known as 'tard' (coextensiveness) used in Islamic jurisprudence to identify the 'illa (cause) of a legal ruling. The author begins by defining tard as the coexistence of an attribute with a ruling in cases of existence only, without considering non-existence. The attribute should not be 'appropriate' (munasib) or 'similar' to the ruling; it is simply a constant concomitant. The comparison must cover all cases except the disputed case (the branch). Some usulis restrict tard to a single case, but the author notes this would turn it into dawaran (coextensiveness in both existence and non-existence), which is different. The difference is clarified: dawaran compares the attribute with the ruling in both existence and non-existence, while tard only in existence. The author then presents a major objection to tard: if coextensiveness means that wherever the attribute is found the ruling is found, then to prove causality we must first establish that the ruling accompanies the attribute in the branch, leading to circular reasoning (dawr). Al-Qarafi's response is presented: tard infers by comparing the attribute with the ruling in all cases except the branch, so no circularity occurs. Another objection is that coextensiveness can exist without causality, as in the examples of a limit with other limits, substance with accident, or Divine essence with attributes — they coexist but are not causally related. The response is that occasional disjunction does not disprove indication, just as clouds are a sign of rain even when rain sometimes doesn't fall. Moreover, all methods of identifying cause (appropriateness, dawaran, efficacy, suggestion) can be disjoined from causality occasionally without losing their indicative value. Finally, the author presents three arguments of those who accept tard. First: if we know the ruling must have a cause and our mind is emptied of other attributes, we infer that the observed attribute is the cause; this is criticized as unrealistic for a mujtahid. Second: incomplete induction (istiqra' naqis) shows that the rare follows the common, so if the attribute accompanies the ruling in most cases, it likely does in the branch. Third: an example about a horse is introduced but cut off.

Al-Qarāfī's Defense of Ṭard Against Circularity
Al-Qarāfī's rebuttal of circularity against ṭard

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how al-Qarāfī resolves the circularity objection against ṭard by restricting its definition to exclude the disputed branch.

Core Idea
Ṭard avoids circularity by excluding the branch case from the evidence.

In uṣūl al-fiqh, the method of ṭard (coincidence) is used to prove that a description is the cause of a ruling by showing the description accompanies the ruling in all cases. However, critics object that this leads to circular reasoning: the ruling in the branch is used to prove the ṭard, and the ṭard proves the ruling. Al-Qarāfī, a commentator on al-Maḥṣūl, rebuts this by clarifying that ṭard is defined as concomitance in all cases except the branch. This lesson unpacks his response and its logical significance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Read the first part of the passage: 'When the ruling is established in the branch, that description is the cause. And we prove its causality by its being coincident. This necessitates circularity.' Understand the logical structure: if we use ṭard to prove causality, and ṭard depends on the ruling, then ruling and cause become interdependent.
2
Locate al-Qarāfī's response
The response states: 'Ṭard is inference by the concomitance of the description with the ruling in all instances except the instance of the branch, so circularity does not arise.' Notice the key phrase 'except the instance of the branch'.
This is a definitional refinement.
3
Analyze how the refinement avoids circularity
By excluding the branch from the evidence for ṭard, the ruling in the branch is not used as a premise. The ṭard is established independently from the branch's ruling, so there is no dependence loop.
The proof of causality does not rely on the very conclusion it aims to prove.
4
Assess the strength of the rebuttal
Consider whether this response fully resolves the objection. Al-Qarāfī redefines ṭard so that it only covers cases where the ruling is already known, leaving the branch as the test case. This is a standard and accepted defense in uṣūl al-fiqh.
The debate rests on the precise definition of ṭard.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإذا ما ثبت الحكم في الفرع ، يكون ذلك الوصف علة . وأثبتنا عليته بكونه مطرداً . لزم الدور . ويورد القرافي من شراح الحصول رداً على هذا الاعتراض بأن الطرد هو الاستدلال بمقارنة الوصف للحكم في جميع الصور ما عدا صورة الفرع فلا يلزم الدور(١) .
When the ruling is established in the branch, that description is the cause. And we prove its causality by its being coincident. This necessitates circularity. Al-Qarāfī, among the commentators of al-Maḥṣūl, responds to this objection: ṭard is inference by the concomitance of the description with the ruling in all instances except the instance of the branch, so circularity does not arise.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exchange regarding the method of ṭard (coincidence) in establishing causality. The author first presents the objection: if one uses ṭard to prove that a description is the cause of a ruling in a branch case, then proving the ṭard itself relies on the ruling, leading to a circular argument (dawr). Al-Qarāfī refutes this by clarifying that ṭard is not simply the mere presence of the description with the ruling in all cases; rather, it is the inference based on the description's association with the ruling in all cases excluding the branch under dispute. Therefore, the ruling in the branch is not used as evidence for the ṭard, thus avoiding the circle. This is a standard defense of ṭard in uṣūl al-fiqh.

Key Takeaway

Defending Ṭard Against the Objection of Non-Causal Correlation
Author raises and rebuts the objection that ṭard can occur without causality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to articulate the objection against ṭard as a method for establishing causality and explain why occasional exceptions do not negate its indicative value.

Core Idea
Correlation alone does not prove causation, but consistent correlation remains a valid indicator.

In usul al-fiqh, the method of ṭard (coincidence) is used to identify the effective cause ('illa) of a ruling. Critics object that correlation can exist without causation, giving examples like substance and accident. This passage presents that objection and the author's robust response, defending ṭard as a valid indicator despite occasional exceptions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Objection
Find the main claim against ṭard: that it can occur without causality. Note the examples given: limit with limits, substance with accident, God's essence with attributes.
These examples show correlation without causal relation.
2
Understand the Rebuttal's Strategy
The author does not deny that ṭard can occur without causality, but argues that occasional detachment does not invalidate its indicative value. The cloud-rain analogy illustrates that a sign can be reliable even if it sometimes fails.
The rebuttal shifts the burden: exceptions do not disprove general reliability.
3
Extend to Other Methods
The author notes that other methods (munāsaba, dawarān, ta'thīr, īmā') are similarly subject to occasional detachment, yet they remain valid indicators of causality.
This strengthens the defense by showing consistency across methods.
4
Evaluate the Debate
Assess whether the rebuttal fully counters the objection. The objector seeks to disprove causality, while the author redefines ṭard as an indicator that does not require invariable causality.
The key move is distinguishing between absolute proof and indicative value.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قد يحدث الإطراد بدون وجود العلة كالحد مع الحدود . والجوهر مع العرض ، وذات الله مع صفاته ، فإن كلاً منهما مقارن الآخر ولا علية بينهما . ويرد على هذا بأن غاية هذا الاعتراض هو حصول الطرد منفكاً عن العلية في بعض الصور ، وليس في هذا مطلقاً قدح في دلالته على العلية فالغيم دليل المطر ، ثم عدم نزول المطر في بعض الصور الصور لا يقدح في كونه دليلاً . وعلاوة على ذلك فإن المسالك المختلفة من مناسبة ودوران وتأثير وإيماء ، قد تنفك عن العلية ولا يقدح في كونها دليلاً على العلية(٢) .
Coincidence may occur without the existence of the cause, like limit with limits, substance with accident, and God's essence with His attributes—each of them accompanies the other with no causality between them. It is responded to this that the point of this objection is the occurrence of ṭard detached from causality in some instances, and this does not absolutely impair its indication of causality, for clouds are a sign of rain, then the absence of rain in some instances does not impair its being a sign. Moreover, the various methods—suitability, concomitance, effect, and indication—may be detached from causality and that does not impair their being indications of causality.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage presents a criticism against ṭard (coincidence) as a method of proving causality. The critic argues that correlation (ṭard) can exist without a causal relationship, providing examples: the concept of limit with other limits, substance with accident, and God's essence with His attributes—all are correlated but not causally connected. The author responds by stating that the objection only shows that ṭard sometimes occurs without causality, but this does not nullify its value as an indicator. Using an analogy: clouds are a sign of rain, even if rain does not fall in every instance. Similarly, other methods like munāsaba (suitability), dawarān (concomitance), ta'thīr (effect), and īmā' (indication) may also be detached from causality, yet they remain valid indicators. Thus, occasional exceptions do not refute the general indicative value of ṭard.

Definition of Dawarān and Its Elements
Author defines dawarān as the third method, describing its components and giving an example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define al-dawarān, identify its three elements (madār, dā'ir, dawarān), and apply the method of concomitant existence and non-existence to a fiqh example.

Core Idea
Dawarān proves causation by observing that a ruling exists with a description and ceases when it ceases.

In uṣūl al-fiqh, scholars use multiple methods to identify the effective cause ('illah) behind a ruling. One of these is dawarān, or concomitance. In this lesson, we examine how dawarān works by breaking down its components and applying them to a concrete fiqh example. The passage from your textbook defines dawarān and includes a clarifying quotation from al-Iṣfahānī's commentary on al-Maḥṣūl.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of dawarān
Dawarān is defined as the revolving of the cause with the effect in existence and non-existence. This means that when the cause exists, the effect exists; when the cause is absent, the effect is absent. The uṣūl scholars also call it 'jaryān' or 'ṭard wa 'aks'.
2
Identify the three elements: madār, dā'ir, dawarān
The madār is the description claimed to be the cause; the dā'ir is the ruling claimed to be the effect; and dawarān is the relationship of co-occurrence between them. Al-Iṣfahānī emphasizes that these three are necessary for the method.
Madār = pivot; dā'ir = revolving; dawarān = concomitance.
3
Apply the condition of ordering existence upon existence
Al-Iṣfahānī states: 'There must be ordering of existence upon existence and non-existence upon non-existence.' This means that the presence of the description must coincide with the presence of the ruling, and the absence of the description with the absence of the ruling, in a consistent manner.
This condition distinguishes dawarān from mere coincidence.
4
Analyze the fiqh example: grape juice and intoxication
The example: grape juice before intoxication is halal by consensus; after intoxication, it becomes haram. The description (intoxication) is the madār, the ruling (prohibition) is the dā'ir, and the co-occurrence of existence and non-existence shows that intoxication is the cause of prohibition.
This is a classic example used in uṣūl to illustrate dawarān.
5
Distinguish dawarān from simple correlation
Dawarān requires both positive and negative correlation: the ruling must follow the description in both existence and non-existence. Simple correlation only observes presence; dawarān also checks absence to confirm causation.
This is why the definition explicitly mentions 'existence and non-existence'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جـ ـ المسلك الثالث . الدوران : الدوران يعرف بأنه دوران العلة مع المعلول وجوداً وعدماً . ويعبر الأصوليون عنه « بالجريان » أو « بالطرد والعكس » وهو أن يوجد الحكم بوجود الوصف ويرتفع بارتفاعه . فيعلم أن هذا الوصف علة ذلك الحكم والوصف يسمى مداراً والحكم يسمى دائراً . يقول الأصفهاني في شرحه على المحصول « الدوران يستلزم المدار والدائر ـ فالمدار هو المدعى عليه والدائر هو المدعي معلوليته . ولا بد من ترتيب الوجود على الوجود والعدم على العدم »(٣) فعناصر هذا المسلك إذن ثلاثة : المدار والدائر والدوران . ومثاله : أن عصير العنب قبل أن يدخله الأسكار ليس بحرام إجماعاً فإذا ما دخله الأسكار كان حراماً إجماعاً .
C—The third method: Dawarān. Dawarān is defined as the revolving of the cause with the effect in existence and non-existence. The scholars of uṣūl express it as 'al-jaryān' or 'al-ṭard wa al-'aks,' and it is that the ruling exists with the existence of the description and ceases with its cessation. So it is known that this description is the cause of that ruling. The description is called madār (the pivot) and the ruling is called dā'ir (the revolving). Al-Iṣfahānī says in his commentary on al-Maḥṣūl: 'Dawarān necessitates the pivo and the revolving—the pivot is that to which causality is attributed, and the revolving is that which is claimed to be the effect. And there must be ordering of existence upon existence and non-existence upon non-existence.' Thus, the elements of this method are three: the pivot, the revolving, and the revolving relationship. Its example: Grape juice before intoxication enters it is not forbidden by consensus, and when intoxication enters it, it becomes forbidden by consensus.
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage defines dawarān (concomitance) as the third method of establishing causality after munāsaba and ṭard. Dawarān is defined as the co-occurrence of the cause and effect in both existence and non-existence. The uṣūl scholars also call it 'jaryān' or 'ṭard wa 'aks.' It means that when the description exists, the ruling exists; when the description is absent, the ruling is absent. Thus, the description is identified as the cause. The description is termed 'madār' (pivot) and the ruling 'dā'ir' (revolving). Al-Iṣfahānī's quotation emphasizes the necessity of proper ordering: existence must follow existence, and non-existence must follow non-existence. The three elements are: the madār (the pivot—the claimed cause), the dā'ir (the revolving—the effect), and the relationship of dawarān. An example is given from fiqh: grape juice before intoxication is permissible by consensus; after becoming intoxicating, it becomes forbidden by consensus. The change in ruling exactly follows the presence or absence of intoxication, proving intoxication is the cause of prohibition.

Key Terms
الدوران (al-dawarān)
المدار (al-madār)
الدائر (al-dā'ir)

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Existence, non-existence, and concomitance

Key Takeaway
Dawarān is a method in uṣūl al-fiqh that identifies the cause of a ruling by observing the concomitance of the description and ruling in both existence and non-existence.

Consider the case: 'Dry dates are halal; when they become intoxicating, they become haram.' Identify the madār, dā'ir, and dawarān relationship in this example.
?
Explain why the grape juice example is a valid application of dawarān. Be sure to mention both the positive and negative correlation (existence and non-existence) as required by the method.

Defining Dawaran: Co-rotation and Empirical Observation
Author introduces dawaran with example and equates it to tajriba.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define al-dawaran and explain its relationship to empirical observation (tajriba) in usul al-fiqh.

Core Idea
Dawaran is co-rotation, which usulis equate with empirical observation to infer causality.

In this lesson, we explore the core concept of al-dawaran – the constant conjunction of a description and a ruling. The author begins by illustrating dawaran with a classic usul example: intoxication and prohibition. He then equates dawaran with experimental observation (tajriba), noting that many medical rules are founded on this same co-rotation. Understanding this foundational concept is essential before engaging with the scholarly disagreements about its epistemic value.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core example of dawaran
Read the opening Arabic: what description (wasf) and ruling (hukm) co-rotate? Note the pattern: when intoxication exists, prohibition exists; when intoxication is absent, prohibition is absent.
The example is: 'intoxication' (al-iskaar) and 'prohibition' (al-tahrim).
2
Trace the author's claim about dawaran and tajriba
The author equates dawaran with tajriba. Al-Naysaburi's medical example supports this: diarrhea, heat, and cold co-rotate with certain foods. This shows that dawaran is empirical observation.
Experience (tajriba) when repeated can yield certainty, as with cutting the head leading to death.
3
Distinguish dawaran from mere correlation
Note that dawaran is presented as a source of knowledge for causality. However, later we will see that some scholars question whether correlation implies causation. For now, understand the basic concept.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذهب عنه الأسکار ذهب عنه التحريم . فلما دار التحريم مع الأسکار وجوداً وعدماً ، ثبت لنا أن الأسکار علة التحريم . والدوران يستند إلى التجربة . بل إن الأصوليين يعتبرونهما شيئاً واحداً « الدورانات عين التجربة . وقد تكثر التجربة فتفيد القطع ، وقد لا تصل إلى ذلك ، قطع الرأس مستلزم قطعاً للموت ونظنه مع السم »(۱) ويقول رضا الدين النيسابوري « الدورانات الدالة على علية المدار كثيرة جداً تفوت الإحصاء ـ وذلك لأن جملة كثيرة من قواعد علم الطب إنما ثبتت بالتجربة وهي الدوران بعينه . وذلك كالإسهال والسخونة والبرودة فإنها تدور مع تناول بعض الأدوية والأغذية وجوداً وعدماً » (٢) . وينقسم الأصوليون في اعتبار الدوران موصلاً إلى اليقين أو الظن أو لا يفيد يقيناً ولا ظناً إلى أربعة أقسام : ١ - القسم الأول : وهو يرى أن الدوران يفيد اليقين ويمثل هذا القسم أصوليو المعتزلة ، وقد قالوا إن الدوران يؤدي إلى القطع بالعلية وأنه لا دليل فوقه . وبعض الأشاعرة الذين قالوا بأنه إذا كثرت التجربة أفادت القطع أو اليقين . ويقول القرافي « الدوران يفيد اليقين عقلاً كدوران العلم مع العالمية - وعادة كدوران الموت مع قطع الرأس » ويقول كثير من الجدليين(۳) « إنه من أقوى ما يثبت به العلل »(٤) . ٢ - القسم الثاني : ويمثله معظم أصولي الأشاعرة وهم يرون أن الدوران يؤدي إلى الظن مهما كثرت التجربة ، وذلك بشروط أهمها عدم المزاحم والسلامة عن المعارض والتكرار(٥) . ويعللون هذا بأن العلة لا توجب الحكم بذاتها ولكن هي « علامة » منصوبة فإذا دار الوصف مع الحكم وجوداً وعدماً غلب على الظن كونه معرفاً لها . يقول رضا الدين النيسابوري « إننا إذا وجدنا دوراناً لا يمنعه مانع من القيود التي تمنع الدوران ـ فإنه يغلب على الظن كونه فرداً من تلك الدورانات ، وكون ذلك المدار من تلك الدورانات ، فتحصل عليه الظن بعليته إلحاقاً للفرد بالأعم الأغلب . وذلك لأنه لما علمنا كون أهل الحبشة سوداً والصقالبة بيضاً ، غلب على الظن كون الحبشي المذكور أسود ، وكون الصقلبي المذكور أبيض »(۱) . ٣ - القسم الثالث : يرى أن الدوران شرط في صحة العلية ، وليس دليلاً على صحتها . يقول ابن برهان « الطرد عندنا شرط صحة العلة وليس دليلاً على صحتها ، أما الإنعكاس فليس بشرط لصحة العلية(٢) . ٤ - القسم الرابع : وهم الذين يرون أن الدوران لا يدل على العلية واستندوا في هذا إلى ما يأتي : أولاً ـ « أن الدوران لو كان دليل العلة ، لكان كل مدار علة للدائر معه ولكن التالي باطل فالمقدم مثله » وإثبات بطلان الدوران أن هناك حالات يتحقق فيها الدوران ولم تتحقق فيها العلية وأوضحها هي . ١ ـ أن العلة نفسها تدور مع المعلول نفسه وجوداً وعدماً ، مع أن المعلول ليس بعلة لعلة قطعاً . ٢ ـ الجوهر والعرض متلازمان نفياً وإثباتاً ، مع أن أحدهما ليس علة في وجود الآخر . ٣ ـ ذات الله وصفاته متلازمان . وكل صفة من صفاته تلازم سائر الصفات ولكن لا علية بينهما . ٤ ـ المتضايفان كالأبوة والنبوة متلازمان وجوداً وعدماً ، مع أن أحدهما ليس علة في وجود الآخر .
When intoxication was present, prohibition was present. Since prohibition co-rotates with intoxication in existence and non-existence, it is established for us that intoxication is the cause of prohibition. Co-rotation is based on experience. Indeed, the usulis consider them one and the same: 'Co-rotations are identical to experience. Experience may be repeated and yield certainty, or it may not reach that level; cutting the head necessarily entails death, and we assume it with poison.' And Rida al-Din al-Naysaburi says: 'The co-rotations indicating the causality of the co-rotating thing are very numerous, beyond count – because many rules of the science of medicine are only established through experience, which is exactly co-rotation. Such as diarrhea, heat, and cold, which co-rotate with the consumption of certain medicines and foods in existence and non-existence.' And the usulis divide regarding the consideration of co-rotation as leading to certainty, or to probability, or as yielding neither certainty nor probability, into four sections: 1 – The first section: It holds that co-rotation yields certainty, and this section is represented by the usulis of the Mu'tazila, who said that co-rotation leads to certainty of causality and that there is no proof above it. And some Ash'aris who said that if experience is repeated, it yields certainty or conviction. Al-Qarafi says: 'Co-rotation yields certainty rationally, like the co-rotation of knowledge with knownness – and customarily, like the co-rotation of death with cutting the head.' And many of the dialecticians say: 'It is among the strongest means by which causes are established.' 2 – The second section: Represented by most Ash'ari usulis, who hold that co-rotation leads to probability no matter how much experience is repeated, under conditions most importantly the absence of an impediment, safety from counterevidence, and repetition. They justify this by saying that the cause does not necessitate the ruling by itself, but it is an appointed 'sign'; so when the description co-rotates with the ruling in existence and non-existence, it becomes probable that it is a signifier of it. Rida al-Din al-Naysaburi says: 'When we find a co-rotation that no impediment of the conditions that prevent co-rotation prevents, then it becomes probable that it is an instance of those co-rotations, and that this co-rotating thing is among those co-rotations, so we obtain probability of its causality by assimilating the individual to the more common and frequent. That is because when we know that the people of Abyssinia are black and the Slavs are white, it becomes probable that a particular Abyssinian is black and a particular Slav is white.' 3 – The third section: It holds that co-rotation is a condition for the validity of causality, not a proof of its validity. Ibn Burhan says: 'Correlation (al-tard) is for us a condition of the validity of the cause, not a proof of its validity; as for conversion (al-inkaas), it is not a condition for the validity of causality.' 4 – The fourth section: Those who hold that co-rotation does not indicate causality, and they base this on the following: First – 'If co-rotation were a proof of causality, then every co-rotating thing would be a cause of what co-rotates with it; but the consequent is false, so the antecedent is likewise false.' And the proof of the falsehood of co-rotation is that there are cases in which co-rotation occurs but causality does not, the clearest of which are: 1. The cause itself co-rotates with its effect in existence and non-existence, while the effect is certainly not a cause of the cause. 2. Substance and accident are mutually concomitant in negation and affirmation, while neither is a cause of the existence of the other. 3. The essence of God and His attributes are mutually concomitant, and each attribute of His is concomitant with the rest of the attributes, but there is no causality between them. 4. The two correlatives, like fatherhood and sonship, are mutually concomitant in existence and non-existence, while neither is a cause of the existence of the other.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage classifies the views of Islamic legal theorists (usulis) on the epistemological value of al-dawaran – the co-rotation or constant conjunction of a descriptive attribute (wasf) and a ruling (hukm) in both existence and non-existence. The author begins by illustrating dawaran with an example: when intoxication (al-iskaar) is present, prohibition (al-tahrim) is present; when intoxication disappears, prohibition disappears. This repeated co-rotation indicates that intoxication is the cause ('illa) of prohibition. The passage then notes that dawaran is based on empirical observation (tajriba), and indeed usulis equate dawaran with tajriba. After quoting al-Qarafi and Rida al-Din al-Naysaburi – who note that many medical rules are established through tajriba, which is exactly dawaran – the author presents the fourfold division of opinions.

First division: The Mu'tazila and some Ash'aris hold that dawaran yields certainty (yaqin) regarding causality. For them, when a description constantly co-rotates with a ruling, it is certain that the description is the cause. Al-Qarafi gives the rational example of knowledge co-rotating with knownness (al-'ilm ma'a al-'alamiyya) and the customary example of death co-rotating with cutting the head.

Second division: Most Ash'ari usulis hold that dawaran yields only probability (zann), no matter how many times it repeats, provided there is no impediment (mazahim), no counterevidence (mu'arid), and repetition (takrar). They argue that the cause is not an agent that necessitates the ruling inherently; rather, it is a 'sign' ('alama) appointed by the Lawgiver. Thus, repeated co-rotation gives probable indication of causality, analogous to how repeated observation that Abyssinians are black makes it probable that a particular Abyssinian is black.

Third division: Some, like Ibn Burhan, hold that dawaran is a condition (shart) for the validity of a causal claim, not a proof (dalil) of its validity. That is, for a description to be considered a valid cause, it must co-rotate with the ruling, but co-rotation alone does not prove the description is the cause. He distinguishes between 'al-tard' (correlation: existence of description implies existence of ruling) and 'al-inkaas' (conversion: non-existence of description implies non-existence of ruling); he says al-tard is a condition, al-inkaas is not.

Fourth division: Those who deny that dawaran indicates causality at all. They argue with a reductio: if co-rotation proved causality, then every co-rotating thing would be the cause of what co-rotates with it – which is absurd. They give four counterexamples: (1) the effect co-rotates with the cause, but the effect is not the cause of the cause; (2) substance and accident are always co-present, but neither causes the other; (3) God's essence and attributes are inseparable, yet there is no causation between them; (4) correlatives like fatherhood and sonship always coexist, but neither causes the other. These examples show that constant co-rotation does not entail causality.

The commentary here follows the author's sequential enumeration. Each position is explained in its own terms, and the argument of the fourth group is given in detail with examples from metaphysics and logic.

Key Terms
الدوران
التجربة
العلة
اليقين

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does Rida al-Din al-Naysaburi use as an example to show that dawaran is the same as tajriba?
A) The co-rotation of cutting the head and death.
B) The co-rotation of intoxication and prohibition.
C) The co-rotation of diarrhea, heat, and cold with certain foods.
D) The co-rotation of fatherhood and sonship.

Key Takeaway
Dawaran is the empirical observation of constant conjunction, used in usul to argue for causality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the usul principle that 'the prohibition of traveling during Ramadan co-rotates with the description of being a traveler.' Write a brief co-rotation pattern showing existence and non-existence, and state whether this could be used to argue that traveling is the cause of the prohibition.

The First Three Positions on Dawaran: Certainty, Probability, or Condition
Author lists first three of four positions on dawaran.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the first three positions on the epistemic value of dawaran: yielding certainty (yaqin), probability (zann), or being merely a condition (shart).

Core Idea
Scholars disagree on whether dawaran proves causation certainly, probably, or only conditionally.

The author now presents the classification of usuli views on dawaran. The first three groups represent a spectrum: from the Mu'tazila who claim certainty, to most Ash'aris who grant only probability, to those who treat co-rotation as a condition for causality rather than a proof. Each position has a distinct rationale rooted in their understanding of causality and empirical knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three positions
List the first three positions: (1) dawaran yields certainty (yaqin); (2) dawaran yields only probability (zann); (3) dawaran is a condition (shart) for causality, not a proof.
These correspond to sections 1, 2, and 3 in the text.
2
For each position, note the representative scholars and their key argument
Position 1: Mu'tazila and some Ash'aris – co-rotation leads to certainty. Position 2: Most Ash'aris – probability only, due to conditions (no impediment, no counterevidence, repetition). Position 3: Ibn Burhan – correlation (tard) is a condition, conversion (inkaas) is not.
Al-Qarafi is quoted for position 1, al-Naysaburi for position 2.
3
Understand the second group's conditions
The second group says dawaran yields probability only if there is no impediment (mazahim), no counterevidence (mu'arid), and repetition (takrar). They view the cause as a 'sign' ('alama) appointed by the Lawgiver.
This reflects the Ash'ari epistemology that causation is not necessary but habitual.
4
Compare with the third group's distinction between tard and inkaas
Ibn Burhan holds that al-tard (positive correlation: when the description exists, the ruling exists) is a condition for causality, but al-inkaas (negative correlation: when the description is absent, the ruling is absent) is not required.
This means the third group is more restrictive: they require co-rotation only in one direction for validity, not both.

Al-Qarafi gives the rational example of knowledge co-rotating with knownness (al-'ilm ma'a al-'alamiyya) and the customary example of death co-rotating with cutting the head.

Second division: Most Ash'ari usulis hold that dawaran yields only probability (zann), no matter how many times it repeats, provided there is no impediment (mazahim), no counterevidence (mu'arid), and repetition (takrar). They argue that the cause is not an agent that necessitates the ruling inherently; rather, it is a 'sign' ('alama) appointed by the Lawgiver. Thus, repeated co-rotation gives probable indication of causality, analogous to how repeated observation that Abyssinians are black makes it probable that a particular Abyssinian is black.

Third division: Some, like Ibn Burhan, hold that dawaran is a condition (shart) for the validity of a causal claim, not a proof (dalil) of its validity. That is, for a description to be considered a valid cause, it must co-rotate with the ruling, but co-rotation alone does not prove the description is the cause. He distinguishes between 'al-tard' (correlation: existence of description implies existence of ruling) and 'al-inkaas' (conversion: non-existence of description implies non-existence of ruling); he says al-tard is a condition, al-inkaas is not.

Fourth division: Those who deny that dawaran indicates causality at all. They argue with a reductio: if co-rotation proved causality, then every co-rotating thing would be the cause of what co-rotates with it – which is absurd. They give four counterexamples: (1) the effect co-rotates with the cause, but the effect is not the cause of the cause; (2) substance and accident are always co-present, but neither causes the other; (3) God's essence and attributes are inseparable, yet there is no causation between them; (4) correlatives like fatherhood and sonship always coexist, but neither causes the other. These examples show that constant co-rotation does not entail causality.

The commentary here follows the author's sequential enumeration. Each position is explained in its own terms, and the argument of the fourth group is given in detail with examples from metaphysics and logic.

Key Terms
اليقين
الظن
الشرط
العلة
العلامة

The Fourth Group's Challenge: When Co-rotation Does Not Imply Causation
Author presents fourth group's critique of dawaran with counterexamples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the fourth group's challenge to dawaran and recall the four counterexamples they use to show that co-rotation does not imply causation.

Core Idea
The fourth group proves that constant conjunction is not sufficient for causality, using clear counterexamples from theology and logic.

The fourth and most skeptical group denies that dawaran indicates causality at all. They offer a reductio argument: if co-rotation proved causation, then every co-rotating pair would be cause and effect, which is false. They provide four compelling counterexamples: cause-effect, substance-accident, God's essence and attributes, and correlatives. These examples are drawn from various domains and show the limits of empirical co-rotation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the reductio argument
The fourth group uses a conditional argument: 'If dawaran were a proof of causality, then every co-rotating thing would be a cause of what co-rotates with it.' Then they argue the consequent is false, so the antecedent is false. This is modus tollens.
This is the core logical structure of their objection.
2
List the four counterexamples
The four examples: (1) cause co-rotates with effect, but effect is not cause; (2) substance and accident are concomitant but not cause; (3) God's essence and attributes are concomitant with no causation; (4) correlatives (e.g., fatherhood and sonship) co-rotate but neither causes the other.
These are all cases of constant co-rotation without causality.
3
For each example, explain why co-rotation exists but causality does not
For (1), the effect's existence depends on the cause, but the cause does not depend on the effect. For (2), substance and accident always accompany each other but are distinct categories. For (3), divine attributes are inseparable from the essence but not caused by it. For (4), correlatives like fatherhood and sonship are relational: one cannot exist without the other, but neither produces the other.
The point is that co-rotation is a necessary condition for causality but not sufficient.
4
Evaluate the strength of these counterexamples
These examples are powerful because they are drawn from domains where causality is clearly not present. They force proponents of dawaran to refine their definition – e.g., by adding the condition that the co-rotation must be in both existence and non-existence (which the fourth group also applies) and also that there must be no other factor. The fourth group's examples show that even full co-rotation does not guarantee causality.
Some defenders of dawaran may argue that these cases are not 'real' co-rotation because they involve necessary concomitance rather than contingent conjunction.

القسم الأول : وهو يرى أن الدوران يفيد اليقين ويمثل هذا القسم أصوليو المعتزلة ، وقد قالوا إن الدوران يؤدي إلى القطع بالعلية وأنه لا دليل فوقه . وبعض الأشاعرة الذين قالوا بأنه إذا كثرت التجربة أفادت القطع أو اليقين . ويقول القرافي « الدوران يفيد اليقين عقلاً كدوران العلم مع العالمية - وعادة كدوران الموت مع قطع الرأس » ويقول كثير من الجدليين(۳) « إنه من أقوى ما يثبت به العلل »(٤) . ٢ - القسم الثاني : ويمثله معظم أصولي الأشاعرة وهم يرون أن الدوران يؤدي إلى الظن مهما كثرت التجربة ، وذلك بشروط أهمها عدم المزاحم والسلامة عن المعارض والتكرار(٥) . ويعللون هذا بأن العلة لا توجب الحكم بذاتها ولكن هي « علامة » منصوبة فإذا دار الوصف مع الحكم وجوداً وعدماً غلب على الظن كونه معرفاً لها . يقول رضا الدين النيسابوري « إننا إذا وجدنا دوراناً لا يمنعه مانع من القيود التي تمنع الدوران ـ فإنه يغلب على الظن كونه فرداً من تلك الدورانات ، وكون ذلك المدار من تلك الدورانات ، فتحصل عليه الظن بعليته إلحاقاً للفرد بالأعم الأغلب . وذلك لأنه لما علمنا كون أهل الحبشة سوداً والصقالبة بيضاً ، غلب على الظن كون الحبشي المذكور أسود ، وكون الصقلبي المذكور أبيض »(۱) . ٣ - القسم الثالث : يرى أن الدوران شرط في صحة العلية ، وليس دليلاً على صحتها . يقول ابن برهان « الطرد عندنا شرط صحة العلة وليس دليلاً على صحتها ، أما الإنعكاس فليس بشرط لصحة العلية(٢) . ٤ - القسم الرابع : وهم الذين يرون أن الدوران لا يدل على العلية واستندوا في هذا إلى ما يأتي : أولاً ـ « أن الدوران لو كان دليل العلة ، لكان كل مدار علة للدائر معه ولكن التالي باطل فالمقدم مثله » وإثبات بطلان الدوران أن هناك حالات يتحقق فيها الدوران ولم تتحقق فيها العلية وأوضحها هي . ١ ـ أن العلة نفسها تدور مع المعلول نفسه وجوداً وعدماً ، مع أن المعلول ليس بعلة لعلة قطعاً . ٢ ـ الجوهر والعرض متلازمان نفياً وإثباتاً ، مع أن أحدهما ليس علة في وجود الآخر . ٣ ـ ذات الله وصفاته متلازمان . وكل صفة من صفاته تلازم سائر الصفات ولكن لا علية بينهما . ٤ ـ المتضايفان كالأبوة والنبوة متلازمان وجوداً وعدماً ، مع أن أحدهما ليس علة في وجود الآخر .
When intoxication was present, prohibition was present. Since prohibition co-rotates with intoxication in existence and non-existence, it is established for us that intoxication is the cause of prohibition. Co-rotation is based on experience. Indeed, the usulis consider them one and the same: 'Co-rotations are identical to experience. Experience may be repeated and yield certainty, or it may not reach that level; cutting the head necessarily entails death, and we assume it with poison.' And Rida al-Din al-Naysaburi says: 'The co-rotations indicating the causality of the co-rotating thing are very numerous, beyond count – because many rules of the science of medicine are only established through experience, which is exactly co-rotation. Such as diarrhea, heat, and cold, which co-rotate with the consumption of certain medicines and foods in existence and non-existence.' And the usulis divide regarding the consideration of co-rotation as leading to certainty, or to probability, or as yielding neither certainty nor probability, into four sections: 1 – The first section: It holds that co-rotation yields certainty, and this section is represented by the usulis of the Mu'tazila, who said that co-rotation leads to certainty of causality and that there is no proof above it. And some Ash'aris who said that if experience is repeated, it yields certainty or conviction. Al-Qarafi says: 'Co-rotation yields certainty rationally, like the co-rotation of knowledge with knownness – and customarily, like the co-rotation of death with cutting the head.' And many of the dialecticians say: 'It is among the strongest means by which causes are established.' 2 – The second section: Represented by most Ash'ari usulis, who hold that co-rotation leads to probability no matter how much experience is repeated, under conditions most importantly the absence of an impediment, safety from counterevidence, and repetition. They justify this by saying that the cause does not necessitate the ruling by itself, but it is an appointed 'sign'; so when the description co-rotates with the ruling in existence and non-existence, it becomes probable that it is a signifier of it. Rida al-Din al-Naysaburi says: 'When we find a co-rotation that no impediment of the conditions that prevent co-rotation prevents, then it becomes probable that it is an instance of those co-rotations, and that this co-rotating thing is among those co-rotations, so we obtain probability of its causality by assimilating the individual to the more common and frequent. That is because when we know that the people of Abyssinia are black and the Slavs are white, it becomes probable that a particular Abyssinian is black and a particular Slav is white.' 3 – The third section: It holds that co-rotation is a condition for the validity of causality, not a proof of its validity. Ibn Burhan says: 'Correlation (al-tard) is for us a condition of the validity of the cause, not a proof of its validity; as for conversion (al-inkaas), it is not a condition for the validity of causality.' 4 – The fourth section: Those who hold that co-rotation does not indicate causality, and they base this on the following: First – 'If co-rotation were a proof of causality, then every co-rotating thing would be a cause of what co-rotates with it; but the consequent is false, so the antecedent is likewise false.' And the proof of the falsehood of co-rotation is that there are cases in which co-rotation occurs but causality does not, the clearest of which are: 1. The cause itself co-rotates with its effect in existence and non-existence, while the effect is certainly not a cause of the cause. 2. Substance and accident are mutually concomitant in negation and affirmation, while neither is a cause of the existence of the other. 3. The essence of God and His attributes are mutually concomitant, and each attribute of His is concomitant with the rest of the attributes, but there is no causality between them. 4. The two correlatives, like fatherhood and sonship, are mutually concomitant in existence and non-existence, while neither is a cause of the existence of the other.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage classifies the views of Islamic legal theorists (usulis) on the epistemological value of al-dawaran – the co-rotation or constant conjunction of a descriptive attribute (wasf) and a ruling (hukm) in both existence and non-existence. The author begins by illustrating dawaran with an example: when intoxication (al-iskaar) is present, prohibition (al-tahrim) is present; when intoxication disappears, prohibition disappears. This repeated co-rotation indicates that intoxication is the cause ('illa) of prohibition. The passage then notes that dawaran is based on empirical observation (tajriba), and indeed usulis equate dawaran with tajriba. After quoting al-Qarafi and Rida al-Din al-Naysaburi – who note that many medical rules are established through tajriba, which is exactly dawaran – the author presents the fourfold division of opinions.

First division: The Mu'tazila and some Ash'aris hold that dawaran yields certainty (yaqin) regarding causality. For them, when a description constantly co-rotates with a ruling, it is certain that the description is the cause. Al-Qarafi gives the rational example of knowledge co-rotating with knownness (al-'ilm ma'a al-'alamiyya) and the customary example of death co-rotating with cutting the head.

Second division: Most Ash'ari usulis hold that dawaran yields only probability (zann), no matter how many times it repeats, provided there is no impediment (mazahim), no counterevidence (mu'arid), and repetition (takrar). They argue that the cause is not an agent that necessitates the ruling inherently; rather, it is a 'sign' ('alama) appointed by the Lawgiver. Thus, repeated co-rotation gives probable indication of causality, analogous to how repeated observation that Abyssinians are black makes it probable that a particular Abyssinian is black.

Third division: Some, like Ibn Burhan, hold that dawaran is a condition (shart) for the validity of a causal claim, not a proof (dalil) of its validity. That is, for a description to be considered a valid cause, it must co-rotate with the ruling, but co-rotation alone does not prove the description is the cause. He distinguishes between 'al-tard' (correlation: existence of description implies existence of ruling) and 'al-inkaas' (conversion: non-existence of description implies non-existence of ruling); he says al-tard is a condition, al-inkaas is not.

Fourth division: Those who deny that dawaran indicates causality at all. They argue with a reductio: if co-rotation proved causality, then every co-rotating thing would be the cause of what co-rotates with it – which is absurd. They give four counterexamples: (1) the effect co-rotates with the cause, but the effect is not the cause of the cause; (2) substance and accident are always co-present, but neither causes the other; (3) God's essence and attributes are inseparable, yet there is no causation between them; (4) correlatives like fatherhood and sonship always coexist, but neither causes the other. These examples show that constant co-rotation does not entail causality.

The commentary here follows the author's sequential enumeration. Each position is explained in its own terms, and the argument of the fourth group is given in detail with examples from metaphysics and logic.

Dawaran: Condition or Proof of Causality?
Author presents the third position that dawaran is a condition not proof of causality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between tard and 'aks and evaluate whether dawaran functions as a condition or proof of causality in usul al-fiqh.

Core Idea
Dawaran is a condition, not a proof, of causality: coincidence does not establish causation.

In usul al-fiqh, establishing a causal relationship between an attribute and a ruling requires careful reasoning. One key tool is dawaran – the observation that the cause and effect are always together. However, does this alone prove causation? This passage presents the view that dawaran is merely a condition (shart) for causality, not a proof (dalil). You will learn to differentiate between tard (co-presence) and 'aks (co-absence) and understand why mere coincidence does not suffice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key claim
The passage states that dawaran is a condition (shart) for validity of causality, not a proof (dalil). Note the distinction: a condition is necessary but not sufficient; a proof would be sufficient evidence.
2
Distinguish tard from 'aks
Ibn Burhan assigns different statuses: tard is a condition but not proof; 'aks is not even a condition. Tard = whenever cause exists, effect exists; 'aks = whenever cause is absent, effect is absent.
3
Test with an Islamic example
Consider khamr and intoxication. Co-presence: when khamr is present, intoxication is present. Co-absence: when khamr is absent, intoxication is absent. Does this prove that intoxication is the cause of prohibition? Or only that it is a condition? Think about why jurists require further evidence.
Example adapted from the commentary.
4
Evaluate the logical implication
If dawaran were a proof, any attribute that always accompanies a ruling could be claimed as the cause. For instance, the property of being a liquid always accompanies intoxicants, but that does not make liquidity the cause. This shows why dawaran alone is insufficient.
