٣ ـ القسم الثالث : يرى أن الدوران شرط في صحة العلية ، وليس دليلاً على صحتها . يقول ابن برهان « الطرد عندنا شرط صحة العلة وليس دليلاً على صحتها ، أما الإنعكاس فليس بشرط لصحة العلية »(٢) .
3 - The third section: It holds that concomitance is a condition for the validity of causality, and not a proof of its validity. Ibn Burhan says: 'According to us, co-presence (tard) is a condition for the validity of the cause and not a proof of its validity. As for co-absence ('aks), it is not a condition for the validity of causality.'(2)

Commentary
This passage presents the third position in the debate on whether dawaran (concomitance between a cause and its effect) serves as evidence of causality. The author states that this position views dawaran merely as a condition (shart) for the validity of causality, not as a proof (dalil) of it. He then quotes Ibn Burhan to clarify the distinction between 'tard' (co-presence: whenever the cause is present, the effect is present) and 'aks' (co-absence: whenever the cause is absent, the effect is absent). Ibn Burhan asserts that tard is a condition but not a proof, while 'aks is not even a condition. This highlights a nuanced view in usul al-fiqh: a cause must be co-present with its effect, but mere co-presence does not establish causation; other evidence is needed. The example of khamr (wine) and intoxication: co-presence is necessary but not sufficient to prove that a specific property is the 'illa.

Tanqih al-Manat: Deletion and Determination of the 'Illah
Author defines tanqih al-manat and compares it with sabr and qiyas la farq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define tanqih al-manat, identify its two operations (deletion and determination), and distinguish it from sabr.

Core Idea
Tanqih al-manat is deletion plus determination; sabr is only deletion.

In usul al-fiqh, discovering the effective cause ('illah) of a ruling is critical for analogical reasoning. One method, tanqih al-manat, refines the 'illah by deleting irrelevant descriptions and then determining the true cause. This lesson unpacks the definition from classical scholars and clarifies how it differs from related methods like sabr.

Guided Path
1
Identify the method being defined
Locate the term 'tanqih al-manat' in the passage. Note its linguistic meaning (refinement) and its technical application to the 'illah (the place of attachment).
Linguistic analysis helps ground the technical term.
2
Extract the two operations
From al-Subki's definition, identify the two core operations: hazf (deletion) of irrelevant descriptions and ta'yin (determination) of the true 'illah. The passage explicitly states that tanqih consists of these two steps.
Some usulis emphasize that deletion comes before determination.
3
Compare with sabr
Al-Mahalli draws a clear line: sabr is only deletion without determination, whereas tanqih adds the determination step. The passage shows that al-Razi equated them, but al-Mahalli disagrees.
This distinction affects how a jurist verifies the 'illah.
4
Consider the relation to qiyas la farq
The passage mentions that some usulis mix tanqih with qiyas la farq (analogy of no difference). Although the text is cut off, note that both methods aim to isolate the 'illah, but qiyas la farq argues from the absence of a distinguishing factor.
Later scholars differentiate them more rigorously.
5
Summarize the scholarly disagreement
Al-Razi treats tanqih and sabr as identical; al-Mahalli separates them. Understand that the disagreement centers on whether mere deletion of unsuitable descriptions suffices or whether positive determination is needed.
This debate has practical implications in ijtihad.

د - المسلك الرابع : تنقيح المناط . يفسر الزركشي في البحر المحيط تنقيح المناط بأن التنقيح في اللغة التهذيب والتمييز ، فاذا قيل كلام منقح - أي لا حشو فيه - والمناط هو العلة « والمناط في الأصل اسم مكان النوط ( أي التعليق ) من ناطه به إذا علقه عليه وربطه به - وأطلق على العلة ، لأن الشارع ناط الحكم بها وعلقه عليها » . ويعرف السبكي تنقيح المناط إصطلاحاً بما يأتي « أن يدل نص الظاهر على التعليل بوصف ، فيحذف خصوصه عن الإعتبار ، ويناط الحكم بالاعم ، أو تكون أوصافه في محل الحكم ، فيحذف بعضها عن الإعتبار بالإجتهاد ويناط الحكم بالباقي . وحاصله الإجتهاد في الحذف والتعيين » أو بمعنى أدق يقوم هذا المسلك على عمليتين : الأولى هي الحذف والثانية هي التعين . أي على القائس حذف ما لا يصلح للعلية من أوصاف المحل ، ثم يعين العلة من بين ما تبقى . ويمزج بعض الأصوليين تنقيح المناط بمسلكين آخرين - هما السبر وقياس لا فارق - فالرازي يعتبر تنقيح المناط والسبر شيئاً واحداً . ولا يوافق الجلال المحلى على هذا في شرحه لجمع الجوامع . بل يرى أن السبر هو حذف الأوصاف غير المطلوبة وعدم تعيين الباقي للعلة . بينما تنقيح المناط هو حذف وتعيين . فهما يتفقان في المرحلة الأولى ويزداد التنقيح مرحلة . أما عن تنقيح المناط وقياس لا فارق فان الرازي ويتابعه البيضاوي
D - The Fourth Path: Tanqih al-Manat. Al-Zarkashi explains in al-Bahr al-Muhit that tanqih linguistically means refinement and discrimination. So when one says 'refined speech' it means no verbiage in it. And al-Manat is the 'illah. 'Al-Manat originally is a noun for the place of suspension (i.e., attachment) from 'natahu bihi' meaning he hung it on it and tied it to it. And it was applied to the 'illah because the Lawgiver suspended the ruling on it and attached it to it.' And al-Subki defines tanqih al-manat terminologically as: 'That the apparent text indicates the attachment of the ruling to a description, so its particularity is removed from consideration, and the ruling is attached to the more general; or that its descriptions are in the locus of the ruling, so some of them are removed from consideration by ijtihad, and the ruling is attached to the remainder. Its gist is ijtihad in deletion and determination.' Or in a more precise sense, this path consists of two operations: the first is deletion, and the second is determination. That is, the one drawing the analogy must delete the descriptions that are not suitable for causality from the descriptions of the locus, then determine the 'illah from among what remains. And some usulis mix tanqih al-manat with two other paths: sabr and qiyas la farq. Al-Razi considers tanqih al-manat and sabr to be one and the same. Al-Mahalli does not agree with this in his commentary on Jam' al-Jawami'. Rather, he holds that sabr is the deletion of non-desired descriptions without determining the remainder as the 'illah, while tanqih al-manat is deletion and determination. Thus they agree in the first stage, and tanqih adds a stage. As for tanqih al-manat and qiyas la farq, al-Razi and followed by al-Baydawi...
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage introduces the fourth method of discovering the 'illah: tanqih al-manat (refinement of the attachment point). The author begins with the linguistic explanation from al-Zarkashi: 'tanqih' means refinement and purification, like refined speech without redundancy. 'Manat' etymologically means the place of hanging—the point to which something is attached. In usul, it refers to the 'illah because the Lawgiver attaches the ruling to it. Then al-Subki's technical definition is given: when a text indicates a description as the 'illah but the description is too specific, one may delete that specificity and attach the ruling to a more general concept; or when multiple descriptions exist in the locus, some are deleted by ijtihad and the ruling is attached to the remaining ones. Thus tanqih al-manat involves two steps: deletion (hazf) and determination (ta'yin). The author then distinguishes tanqih from sabr and qiyas la farq. Al-Razi equates tanqih and sabr, but al-Mahalli disagrees: sabr only deletes unsuitable descriptions without determining which is the 'illah, whereas tanqih adds the determination step. They share the deletion phase but tanqih goes further. Finally, the author notes the relationship with qiyas la farq, citing al-Razi and al-Baydawi, but the text is cut off.

Key Terms
تنقيح المناط (tanqih al-manat): Refinement of the 'illah by deleting irrelevant descriptions and determining the actual cause. [Note: Some early usulis did not distinguish it from sabr; later scholars like al-Mahalli differentiated.]
المناط (al-manat): The 'illah or cause to which the ruling is attached; literally 'place of suspension'. [Note: In logical contexts, manat can refer to the subject of judgment.]
السبر (al-sabr): Testing: deleting unsuitable descriptions without affirming the remaining as 'illah. [Note: In some texts, sabr is synonymous with tanqih; here they are distinguished.]
قياس لا فارق (qiyas la farq): Analogy of no difference: claiming that there is no distinguishing factor between two cases. [Note: Another method of 'illah discovery, often contrasted with tanqih.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Mahalli, how does tanqih al-manat differ from sabr?
A) Tanqih deletes descriptions while sabr does not
B) Tanqih includes both deletion and determination, while sabr only deletes
C) Sabr determines the 'illah but tanqih does not
D) They are exactly the same according to al-Mahalli

Key Takeaway
Tanqih al-manat refines the 'illah by deleting irrelevant descriptions and then determining the true cause, whereas sabr stops at deletion without determination.

Distinguishing Tanqih al-Manat from Sabr and Qiyas la Farq
Author compares tanqih al-manat with sabr and qiyas la farq, clarifying differences and objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish Tanqih al-Manat from Sabr and Qiyas la Farq based on their definitions and identify which method is more general.

Core Idea
Tanqih al-Manat adds determination of cause, making it more comprehensive than Sabr and Qiyas la Farq.

In this passage, the author clarifies how Tanqih al-Manat relates to two other methods of identifying the ‘illah (cause) in qiyas. While some scholars conflate them, careful analysis reveals key differences in scope and procedure. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for correctly applying the methods of ‘illah verification in usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three methods
The passage discusses Tanqih al-Manat, Sabr, and Qiyas la Farq. Note that some usulis mix them, but the author distinguishes them. Read the definitions given by al-Mahalli and others.
2
Compare Sabr and Tanqih
According to al-Mahalli, Sabr only deletes unsuitable attributes without determining the remaining as cause, while Tanqih adds determination. They agree in the first stage (deletion), but Tanqih has an extra stage of determination.
3
Compare Qiyas la Farq and Tanqih
Al-Razi and al-Baydawi define Tanqih as attaching the branch to the root by eliminating difference. Some object that this is not Tanqih because it lacks refinement of the cause. Tanqih requires knowledge of the common cause, while Qiyas la Farq only asserts no relevant difference.
4
Conclude the generality
The author concludes that Tanqih is more general (a'amm) than both Sabr and Qiyas la Farq, because it includes both deletion and determination, while the others lack either determination or knowledge of the cause.
Generality here means Tanqih encompasses the functions of the other two and adds more.

ويمزج بعض الأصوليين تنقيح المناط بمسلكين آخرين - هما السبر وقياس لا فارق - فالرازي يعتبر تنقيح المناط والسبر شيئاً واحداً . ولا يوافق الجلال المحلى على هذا في شرحه لجمع الجوامع . بل يرى أن السبر هو حذف الأوصاف غير المطلوبة وعدم تعيين الباقي للعلة . بينما تنقيح المناط هو حذف وتعيين . فهما يتفقان في المرحلة الأولى ويزداد التنقيح مرحلة . أما عن تنقيح المناط وقياس لا فارق فان الرازي ويتابعه البيضاوي يعرفان تنقيح المناط بأنه الحاق الفرع بالأصل بإلغاء الفارق . بأن يثبت القائس أنه لا فرق بين الأصل والفرع إلا كذا . وذلك لا مدخل له في الحكم البتة ، فيلوم اشتراكهما في الحكم الموجب له . واعترض بعض الأصوليين على أن إلغاء الفارق بين الأصل والفرع ليس هو تنقيح المناط ، لأن في التنقيح تهذيباً للعلة ، بينما إلغاء الفارق يكون من غير معرفة العلة المشتركة . فتنقيح المناط إذن أعم من قياس لا فارق ، كما أنه أعم من السبر
And some usulis mix Tanqih al-Manat with two other methods: al-Sabr and Qiyas la Farq. Al-Razi considers Tanqih al-Manat and Sabr to be the same thing. Al-Jalal al-Mahalli does not agree with this in his commentary on Jam' al-Jawami'. Rather, he holds that Sabr is deletion of unwanted attributes without determining the remaining as cause, while Tanqih al-Manat is deletion and determination. They agree in the first stage, but Tanqih adds a stage. As for Tanqih al-Manat and Qiyas la Farq, al-Razi and al-Baydawi following him define Tanqih al-Manat as attaching the branch to the root by eliminating the difference. That is, the qa'is proves that there is no difference between the root and branch except such-and-such, and that has no bearing on the ruling at all, so they must share the ruling due to the cause. Some usulis objected that eliminating the difference between root and branch is not Tanqih al-Manat, because in Tanqih there is refinement of the cause, while eliminating the difference occurs without knowledge of the common cause. So Tanqih al-Manat is more general than Qiyas la Farq, and also more general than Sabr.
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage compares Tanqih al-Manat with two related methods: Sabr (testing) and Qiyas la Farq (analogy by eliminating difference). The author reports that some usulis mix these, but al-Mahalli distinguishes them: Sabr only deletes unsuitable attributes without determining the cause, while Tanqih adds determination. Thus they share the first step but Tanqih has an extra stage. For Qiyas la Farq, al-Razi and al-Baydawi define it as attaching the branch to the root by eliminating any difference, but some object that this does not involve refining the cause, only showing no difference exists. The author concludes that Tanqih is more general (a'amm) than both, because it includes both deletion and determination, while Sabr lacks determination and Qiyas la Farq may lack knowledge of the cause.

Tanqih al-Manat: Definition, Objections, and Hanafi Distinctions
Author defines tanqih al-manat and analyzes its nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define tanqih al-manat, distinguish it from qiyas according to the Hanafis, and explain its resemblance to the modern elimination method.

Core Idea
Tanqih al-manat refines the cause by eliminating differences, unlike qiyas which only yields probability.

Tanqih al-manat is a sophisticated method in usul al-fiqh for extending rulings. This lesson unpacks its definition, the objection that eliminating differences alone is insufficient, the Hanafi distinction between istidlal and qiyas, and how it parallels modern hypothesis testing.

Guided Path
1
Define tanqih al-manat
The author defines it as attaching a branch case (far') to an original (asl) by eliminating any difference. Identify the core operation: the prover shows that any apparent difference is irrelevant, so both share the ruling.
2
Understand the objection
Some usulis object that merely eliminating differences is not tanqih al-manat because true tanqih requires refining the cause ('illa). Why is this distinction important?
The author responds by saying tanqih is more general than 'qiyas la farq' and sabr.
3
Distinguish Hanafi terminology
The Hanafis call tanqih al-manat 'istidlal' and contrast it with qiyas: qiyas mentions the common attribute (jam') and yields probability (zann); istidlal eliminates differences and yields certainty (qat').
This highlights the epistemological priority of istidlal.
4
Draw the parallel with modern elimination
The author compares tanqih to the scientific method of elimination: list hypotheses, test via experiments, keep the survivor. Notice how both methods rely on eliminating competing explanations to reach a conclusion.
This shows the inductive genius of Muslim jurists.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يعرفان تنقيح المناط بأنه الحاق الفرع بالأصل بإلغاء الفارق . بأن يثبت القائس أنه لا فرق بين الأصل والفرع إلا كذا . وذلك لا مدخل له في الحكم البتة ، فيلوم اشتراكهما في الحكم الموجب له . واعترض بعض الأصوليين على أن إلغاء الفارق بين الأصل والفرع ليس هو تنقيح المناط ، لأن في التنقيح تهذيباً للعلة ، بينما إلغاء الفارق يكون من غير معرفة العلة المشتركة . فتنقيح المناط إذن أعم من قياس لا فارق ، كما أنه أعم من السبر . ويسمي الحنفية تنقيح المناطق استدلالات . ويفرقون بينه وبين القياس ، بأن القياس اسم لما يكون الإلحاق فيه بذكر الجامع . وهذا القياس لا يفيد إلا الظن . والإستدلال ما يكون الإلحاق فيه بالغاء الفارق الذي يفيد القطع ، بل يجري مجرى القطعيات(۱) . وتنقيح المناط يشبه الطريقة السلبية في إثبات الفرض عند المحدثين - وهي طريق الحذف La Methode d'elimination . وهذه الطريقة - في إيجاز - هي أن يكون لدينا عدد من الفروض ، فنضع قائمة لها . ثم نقوم بحذف الفروض التي تناقض التجارب التي نعملها لتحقيق المسألة التي نريد بحثها . ثم نعتبر الفرض الباقي في القائمة هو الفرض الصحيح(۲) . هذه هي بعض طرق العلة أو مسالكها ، نكتفي من مباحث القياس بها في الآونة الحاضرة . وقد أوضح لنا هذا العرض الموجز لبعض مباحث القياس الأصولي عبقرية المسلمين في التوصل إلى المنهج الإستقرائي في أكمل صوره . فقد أقاموا أكبر طرق البحث العلمي عندهم على قانونين طبيعيين - هما قانون العلية وقانون الإطراد في وقوع الحوادث . ثم اشترطوا للعلة شروطاً ، ووضعوا مسالك لها سبقوا بها المحدثين في وضعهم لقوانين الإستقراء وطرقه وأقاموا القياس الأصولي - كما أقام المحدثون الإستقراء - على أساس التجربة واعتبر كثيرون منهم التجربة موصلة إلى اليقين . وقد تبين لنا الآن في جزم وقطع أن القياس الفقهي شيء آخر غير التمثيل الارسططاليسي ، وأنه نتاج إسلامي خالص توصل إليه المسلمون .
They define tanqih al-manat as attaching the branch to the root by eliminating the difference. That is, the prover establishes that there is no difference between the root and the branch except such-and-such, and that has no entry into the ruling at all, so their sharing in the ruling that necessitates it is required. And some of the usulis objected that eliminating the difference between the root and the branch is not tanqih al-manat, because in tanqih there is a refinement of the cause, while eliminating the difference occurs without knowledge of the common cause. So tanqih al-manat is more general than qiyas la farq, and it is also more general than sabr. The Hanafis call tanqih al-manat 'istidlalat'. And they distinguish between it and qiyas, in that qiyas is a name for that in which the attachment is by mentioning the commonality. And this qiyas yields only opinion. And istidlal is that in which the attachment is by eliminating the difference, which yields certainty; indeed it runs in the course of certainties. And tanqih al-manat resembles the negative method in proving the hypothesis among the moderns—the method of elimination. This method—in brief—is that we have a number of hypotheses, we make a list for them. Then we delete the hypotheses that contradict the experiments we perform to investigate the matter we want to study. Then we consider the remaining hypothesis in the list as the correct hypothesis. These are some of the ways or paths of the cause; we suffice with them from the topics of qiyas at the present time. And this brief presentation of some topics of usuli qiyas has shown us the genius of the Muslims in arriving at the inductive method in its most complete form. For they established the greatest methods of scientific research among them upon two natural laws—the law of causality and the law of regularity in the occurrence of events. Then they stipulated conditions for the cause, and laid down paths for it, by which they preceded the moderns in their formulation of the laws and methods of induction, and they established usuli qiyas—just as the moderns established induction—on the basis of experience, and many of them considered experience to lead to certainty. And it has now become clear to us with certainty and finality that fiqh qiyas is something other than Aristotelian tamthil, and that it is a purely Islamic product arrived at by the Muslims.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage defines and analyzes the concept of tanqih al-manat (refinement of the ratio legis), a key method in usul al-fiqh for extending rulings by analogy. The author begins by stating the definition: attaching a branch case (far') to an original case (asl) by eliminating any difference between them. The prover shows that any apparent difference is irrelevant to the ruling, so both cases must share the same ruling. An objection from some usulis is then noted: they argue that merely eliminating differences is not tanqih al-manat, because tanqih properly involves refining or purifying the cause ('illa), whereas eliminating differences can be done without knowing the shared cause. The author responds by clarifying that tanqih al-manat is more general than 'qiyas la farq' (analogy without a difference) and also more general than sabr (division). The Hanafis terminology is then introduced: they call tanqih al-manat 'istidlal' (inference) and distinguish it from qiyas. For the Hanafis, qiyas involves explicit mention of the common attribute and yields only probability (zann), while istidlal (tanqih al-manat) eliminates the difference and yields certainty. The author then draws a parallel to the modern scientific method of elimination: making a list of hypotheses, testing them through experiments, and keeping the one that survives. He concludes that this brief overview reveals the genius of Muslim scholars in developing the inductive method based on causality and regularity, anticipating modern induction. Finally, he asserts that fiqh qiyas is distinct from Aristotelian analogy (tamthil) and is an original Islamic achievement.

Key Terms
تنقيح المناط
قياس
استدلال
علة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Tanqih al-manat is a uniquely Islamic method of legal reasoning that refines the cause by eliminating differences, achieving certainty, and anticipating modern scientific elimination.

Five Types of Jami' in Qiyas al-Gha'ib 'ala al-Shahid
Classification of five types of jami' in Ash'ari theological analogy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the five types of jami' in Ash'ari qiyas al-gha'ib 'ala al-shahid and apply the correct type to a theological argument.

Core Idea
Ash'ari theologians expanded qiyas beyond legal cause to include condition, proof, definition, and reality.

In usul al-fiqh, analogical reasoning (qiyas) relies solely on the cause ('illa) as the unifying factor between the original case and the new case. However, Ash'ari mutakallimun, when reasoning about unseen divine attributes from the witnessed world, employed four additional types of jami'. This lesson unpacks these five types, enabling you to recognize and evaluate theological arguments that use qiyas al-gha'ib 'ala al-shahid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core components of qiyas al-gha'ib 'ala al-shahid
In this method, the witnessed (shahid) is the root (asl), the unseen (gha'ib) is the branch (far'), and the unifying factor (jami') connects them. Understand that this is distinct from usuli qiyas, which only accepts 'illa as the jami'.
The passage states that usulis only use jami' bil-'illa, while mutakallimun add shart, dalil, hadd, and haqiqa.
2
Analyze jami' bil-'illa (unification by cause)
The example given: 'being a knower' in the witnessed is caused by knowledge. Since the rational cause necessitates its effect, the unseen knower (God) must also have knowledge. This is the standard Ash'ari proof for divine attributes.
The cause here is not a legal cause but a rational one that entails its effect.
3
Analyze jami' bil-shart (unification by condition)
Knowledge is conditioned by life in the witnessed; therefore, the condition (life) must also apply in the unseen. Thus, if God knows, He must be living.
Condition is an existential prerequisite, not a cause.
4
Analyze jami' bil-dalil (unification by proof)
Features like origination (huduth), particularization (takhsis), and judgments (ahkam) in the witnessed serve as proofs of power, will, and knowledge. These proofs must indicate the same attributes in the unseen.
The proof (dalil) is an evidential sign, not a cause or condition.
5
Analyze jami' bil-hadd wa al-haqiqa (unification by definition and reality)
If we define a knower as 'one who has knowledge' in the witnessed, that definition must hold for the unseen. The passage notes a theological disagreement on whether hadd and haqiqa are identical.
Definition and reality may be considered the same or distinct; the text leaves the question open.

Source Text

الفَصْلُ الخَامِس
الطرق الإسلامية الأخرى
الطريق الأول : قياس الغائب على الشاهد
عرضنا في الفصل السابق لأهم طريق من طرق الاستدلالات الإسلامية وهو القياس الأصولي . وسنبحث في هذا الفصل بعض الطرق الأخرى التي استخدمها المسلمون في أبحاثهم وأخذوا بها قبل نهاية القرن الخامس الهجري ولم أستطع مع الاسف الشديد التوصل إلى الكتب التي بحثت هذه الطرق ، وهي كتب الباقلاني وإمام الحرمين لظروف مختلفة ولذلك سنبحثها خلال فقرات باقية في كتب المتأخرين . وقد ذكرنا هذا من قبل . أما أصحاب تلك الطرق فهم الاشاعرة ، ومن المحتمل أن يكون المعتزلة استخدموها أيضاً وهذا الاحتمال يصل إليه ابن رشد « وأما المعتزلة فلم يصل إلينا من كتبهم شيء نقف منه على طرقهم التي سلكوها في هذا المعنى ويشبه أن يكون طرقهم من جنس طرق الأشاعرة » وأول هذه الطرق هو قياس الغائب على الشاهد . وهذا القياس هو القياس الأصولي - فالمقيس عليه عند المتكلمين هو الأصل عند الأصوليين - والمقيس هو الفرع - والجامع بين الأصل والفرع أو بين الشاهد والغائب هو العلة عند الأصوليين . ويضيف المتكلمون إلى الجمع بالعلة الجمع بالشرط والدليل والحد والحقيقة ، وهي ما سنبحثها الآن في إيجاز . لأن الأصوليين لم يتكلموا إلا في الجمع بالعلة . أما الجمع بالشرط والدليل والحد والحقيقة ، فلم يبحثوا فيه .
أما الجمع بالعلة عند المتكلمين فيمثلون له ، بأنه إذا كانت العالمية شاهداً فيمن له العلم معللة بالعلم ، وجب أن تكون ذلك في الغائب . وحاصل هذا الجمع أن العلة العقلية تتلازم مع معلولها ولا يجوز تقدير واحد منها دون الآخر . فلو جاز لنا تقدير العالم عالماً دون العلم ، لجاز تقدير العلم من غير أن يتصف محله بكونه عالماً ، وهذا محال لكن الحقيقة أن الوصف يقتضي الصفة كما أن الصفة تقتضي الوصف . فإذا ثبت أن كون العالم عالماً شاهداً معلل بالعلم ، لزم كون الغائب العالم معللاً بالعلم أيضاً . . وعلى هذا الاساس أثبت الصفاتية للغائب الإرادة والكلام وغيرها من الصفات .
أما الجمع بالشرط : فمثاله العلم مشروط بالحياة شاهداً فكذلك غائباً . وتفسير هذا أنه يجب طرد الشرط شاهداً وغائباً . فان كون العالم عالماً ، لما كان مشروطاً بكونه حياً في الشاهد ، وجب طرده في الغائب . أما الجمع بالدليل فيمثلون له بأن الحدوث والتخصيص والأحكام يدل على القدرة والإرادة والعلم شاهداً فيجب طرد ذلك غائباً . وتفسير هذا انه يجب طرد الدليل شاهداً وغائباً ، فان كون التخصيص والأحكام دليلاً على القدرة في الشاهد وجب طرده في الغائب . أما الجمع بالحد والحقيقة فيمثلون له بأنهم إذا حدوا العالم في الشاهد بأنه من له العلم فيجب طرد الحد غائباً . وقد اختلف المتكلمون في الحد والحقيقة : هل هما شيء واحد أم شيئان
Chapter Five: Other Islamic Methods. The First Method: Analogy of the Unseen to the Witnessed. We presented in the previous chapter the most important method of Islamic proofs, which is usuli analogy. In this chapter we will research some other methods that Muslims used in their investigations and adopted before the end of the fifth century AH, and I was unable, with great regret, to find the books that discussed these methods – they are the books of al-Baqillani and Imam al-Haramayn – due to various circumstances, so we will research them through remaining paragraphs in the books of later scholars. We have mentioned this before. The proponents of those methods are the Ash'aris, and it is possible that the Mu'tazila also used them; this possibility is reached by Ibn Rushd: 'As for the Mu'tazila, nothing of their books has reached us by which we could know their methods they followed in this meaning, and it is likely that their methods are of the same kind as the methods of the Ash'aris.' The first of these methods is analogy of the unseen to the witnessed. This analogy is the usuli analogy: the thing measured against, according to the theologians, is the root (asl) according to the usulis, and the thing measured is the branch (far'), and the unifier between the root and the branch, or between the witnessed and the unseen, is the cause ('illa) according to the usulis. The theologians add to unification by cause: unification by condition, proof, definition, and reality, which we will now research briefly. Because the usulis only discussed unification by cause. As for unification by condition, proof, definition, and reality, they did not research it. As for unification by cause according to the theologians, they exemplify it by: if 'being a knower' (al-'alamiyya) is witnessed in one who has knowledge, being caused by knowledge, then it must be so in the unseen. The summary of this unification is that the rational cause is concomitant with its effect, and it is not permissible to posit one without the other. For if it were permissible for us to posit the knower as knowing without knowledge, it would be permissible to posit knowledge without its locus being described as knowing – this is absurd, but the reality is that the description necessitates the attribute just as the attribute necessitates the description. So if it is established that the knower's being knowing in the witnessed is caused by knowledge, it follows that the unseen knower is also caused by knowledge. On this basis, the attribute-asserters affirmed for the unseen: will, speech, and other attributes. As for unification by condition: its example is that knowledge is conditioned by life in the witnessed, so likewise in the unseen. Its explanation is that the condition must be applied consistently in the witnessed and the unseen. For the knower's being knowing, since it was conditioned by his being alive in the witnessed, it must be applied in the unseen. As for unification by proof: they exemplify it by: origination (huduth), particularization (takhsis), and judgments (ahkam) indicate power, will, and knowledge in the witnessed, so that must be applied in the unseen. Its explanation is that the proof must be applied consistently in the witnessed and the unseen. For the fact that particularization and judgments are proof of power in the witnessed necessitates its application in the unseen. As for unification by definition and reality: they exemplify it by: if they define the knower in the witnessed as 'one who has knowledge', the definition must be applied in the unseen. And the theologians disagreed about definition and reality: are they one thing or two?
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage presents the classification of the types of jami' (unifying factor) used in the Ash'ari theological method of qiyas al-gha'ib 'ala al-shahid (analogy from the witnessed to the unseen). The author begins by situating this method within Islamic reasoning, noting that the usuli (juridical) qiyas was discussed earlier, but the mutakallimun (theologians) employed additional forms of unification beyond the cause ('illa). The author first defines the basic structure: the witnessed (shahid) is the root (asl), the unseen (gha'ib) is the branch (far'), and the common factor is the jami'. In usul, the only jami' accepted is 'illa (cause). The mutakallimun, however, add four more types: shart (condition), dalil (proof), hadd (definition), and haqiqa (reality). The author then explains each type with examples.

1. Jami' bil-'illa (unification by cause): The example given is 'being a knower' (al-'alamiyya). In the witnessed, a person is described as a knower because of knowledge (as the cause). It is impossible to have a knower without knowledge or knowledge without a knower; they are necessarily concomitant. Hence, if the cause (knowledge) is present in the witnessed, it must be present in the unseen. This allowed the Ash'aris to affirm divine attributes like will and speech for God (the unseen).

2. Jami' bil-shart (unification by condition): Knowledge is conditioned by life in the witnessed; that is, a knower must be alive. This condition must apply equally to the unseen. So if knowledge is attributed to God, He must be living.

3. Jami' bil-dalil (unification by proof): Certain features in the witnessed, such as temporal origination (huduth), particularization (takhsis), and the presence of judgments (ahkam), serve as proofs of power, will, and knowledge. Since these proofs indicate those attributes in the witnessed, they must indicate the same attributes in the unseen (i.e., God).

4. Jami' bil-hadd wa al-haqiqa (unification by definition and reality): If we define a knower as 'one who has knowledge' in the witnessed, this definition (hadd) or reality (haqiqa) must be transposed to the unseen. So if God is described as a knower, the definition must hold. The author notes a disagreement among theologians about whether hadd and haqiqa are the same or distinct.

The passage thus systematically presents the expanded framework of analogy used by the Ash'ari school, emphasizing that the rational link between the witnessed and the unseen is not limited to efficient causality but includes other relationships such as conditionality, evidential indication, and definitional identity.

Key Terms
الجمع بالعلة
الجمع بالشرط
الجمع بالدليل
الجمع بالحد
الجمع بالحقيقة
قياس الغائب على الشاهد

Checkpoint

A) The usuli only use qiyas on shahid, while mutakallimun use it on gha'ib.
B) The usuli only accept jami' bil-'illa, while mutakallimun also accept jami' bil-shart, dalil, hadd, and haqiqa.
C) The mutakallimun reject qiyas entirely.
D) The usuli use qiyas for fiqh, while mutakallimun use it for kalam.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari mutakallimun expanded the unifying factor in analogy from the witnessed to the unseen beyond legal cause to include condition, proof, definition, and reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Ibn Taymiyya's Division of Critique into Destructive and Constructive Aspects
Author divides Ibn Taymiyya's critique into three parts and two aspects per part.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to outline Ibn Taymiyya's three-part critique of Aristotelian logic and distinguish the destructive and constructive aspects.

Core Idea
Ibn Taymiyya both demolishes and rebuilds: his critique of logic is as constructive as it is destructive.

This passage sets the stage for understanding Ibn Taymiyya's systematic engagement with Aristotelian logic. Rather than simply rejecting it wholesale, Ibn Taymiyya divides his critique into three core areas: definition, proposition, and syllogism. For each area, he operates on two levels: a destructive level (refuting the logicians' claims) and a constructive level (offering an alternative rooted in Quranic reasoning). This framework allows us to see Ibn Taymiyya not as a mere polemicist, but as a methodical critic who builds even as he tears down.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three-part structure of Ibn Taymiyya's critique
The passage explicitly lists definition (al-hadd), proposition (al-qadiyya), and syllogism (al-qiyas) as the three sections. These are the classical pillars of Aristotelian logic.
2
Distinguish the two aspects within each part
Each part has a destructive aspect (al-janib al-hadmi) and a constructive aspect (al-janib al-insha'i). The destructive aspect includes two levels: negative (al-maqam al-salib) and positive (al-maqam al-mujib).
3
Understand the negative and positive levels
The negative level claims that the desired concept cannot be attained without definition; the positive level claims that definition provides knowledge of concepts. Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the negative level is not original, while his critique of the positive level is innovative.
4
Recognize the reason for separating the two levels
Two reasons: each level is critiqued with independent arguments, and the negative level lacks originality while the positive level contains innovative elements. This distinction helps evaluate the originality of his critique.
5
Apply this framework to a concrete example
In Ibn Taymiyya's critique of definition, the destructive part refutes the claim that definition is necessary or sufficient for knowledge, while the constructive part proposes a Quranic concept-based epistemology.

Source Text

قلنا في الفصل السابق إننا سنقسم نقد ابن تيمية للمنطق الأرسططاليسي إلى أقسام ثلاثة : قسم ينقد فيه ابن تيمية الحد - وقسم ينقد فيه القضية - وقسم ينقد فيه القياس . وقلنا إننا سنعرض تحت كل قسم من هذه الأقسام جانبين من جوانب تفكير ابن تيمية - الجانب الأول - هو الهدمي - والجانب الثاني - هو الإنشائي . ويشمل الجانب الأول ما أسماهما ابن تيمية بالمقامين السالب والموجب ويشمل الجانب الثاني آراء ابن تيمية في المنطق القرآني مستخلصة أحياناً من هذين المقامين السالفين ومن كتب أخرى لإبن تيمية . وسنبحث نحن في هذا الفصل القسم الأول من نقد ابن تيمية أي نقد الحد الأرسططاليسي . وينقسم نقد الحد الأرسططاليسي كما قلنا إلى جانبين : ١ - الجانب الهدمي . ٢ - الجانب الإنشائي . ١ - الجانب الهدمي : نقد مبحث الحد الأرسططاليسي . ينقسم هذا الجانب إلى قسمين أو مقامين : مقام سالب ومقام موجب . فالمقام السالب هو « التصور المطلوب لا ينال إلا بالحد » والمقام الموجب هو « الحد يفيد العلم بالتصورات ، ونستطيع فصل مناقشة كل واحد من هذين المقامين عن الآخر لسببين : أولهما ـ أن ابن تيمية ينقد كل مقام من هذين المقامين بحجج مستقلة . ثانيهما أن المقام السالب ليس عملاً مبتكراً . بل كان ابن تيمية مجرد ناقل فيه ، بينما دخلت عناصر مبتكرة في المقام الموجب .
We said in the previous chapter that we will divide Ibn Taymiyya's critique of Aristotelian logic into three sections: a section in which Ibn Taymiyya critiques the definition, a section in which he critiques the proposition, and a section in which he critiques the syllogism. And we said that we will present under each of these sections two aspects of Ibn Taymiyya's thought: the first aspect is the destructive, and the second aspect is the constructive. And the first aspect includes what Ibn Taymiyya called the negative and positive levels, and the second aspect includes Ibn Taymiyya's views on Quranic logic, sometimes extracted from these two aforementioned levels and from other books by Ibn Taymiyya. And we will examine in this chapter the first section of Ibn Taymiyya's critique, i.e., the critique of the Aristotelian definition. And the critique of the Aristotelian definition, as we said, divides into two aspects: 1 - The destructive aspect. 2 - The constructive aspect. 1 - The destructive aspect: Critique of the topic of the Aristotelian definition. This aspect divides into two parts or levels: a negative level and a positive level. The negative level is 'the desired concept is not obtained except through definition,' and the positive level is 'definition yields knowledge of concepts.' And we can separate the discussion of each of these two levels from the other for two reasons: firstly, Ibn Taymiyya critiques each of these two levels with independent arguments. Secondly, the negative level is not an original work; rather, Ibn Taymiyya was merely a transmitter in it, while innovative elements entered into the positive level.
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage is a methodological introduction that sets the structure for discussing Ibn Taymiyya's critique of Aristotelian definition. The author first reminds us of the three-part division of Ibn Taymiyya's critique: definition, proposition, and syllogism. Under each part, two aspects are examined: the destructive (الهدْمي) and the constructive (الإنشائي). The destructive aspect for the definition is further split into two levels: the negative level (المقام السالب), which claims that concepts can only be obtained through definition, and the positive level (المقام الموجب), which claims that definition yields knowledge of concepts. The author gives two reasons for treating these levels separately: each is critiqued with independent arguments, and the negative level is not original—Ibn Taymiyya merely transmits it from skeptics—while the positive level contains innovative elements. This framing helps the reader understand that Ibn Taymiyya's approach is both a refutation of Aristotle's logical framework and an attempt to replace it with a Quranic alternative.

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of the Foundational Premise
Ibn Taymiyya argues the foundational premise of logic is unproven.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument that the foundational premise of logic lacks proof, and identify the author's objection to its self-consistency.

Core Idea
Ibn Taymiyya attacks logic's foundation, but his own weapon may be double-edged.

Ibn Taymiyya launches a frontal assault on the very basis of Aristotelian logic. He claims that the logicians' foundational premise—the need for definition in conceptualization—is not self-evident and lacks proof. If true, this would undermine the entire science of demonstration (burhan). But the author of Manahij Bahth adds a critical remark: the argument may be self-refuting. Let's unpack this exchange and assess its logical strength.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target premise
Find the foundational premise of logic that Ibn Taymiyya is attacking. It is the claim that definitions are necessary for conceptualization, which logicians treat as axiomatic.
The passage refers to 'this negative aspect' (al-maqam al-salib), likely the negative premise about the necessity of definition.
2
Extract Ibn Taymiyya's reasoning
He argues: (1) The premise is not self-evident (badihah). (2) Therefore it requires proof. (3) Logicians have no proof. (4) Hence it is absurd to base burhan on it.
This is a syllogism: if not self-evident, needs proof; no proof; therefore premise is unproven; therefore cannot be foundation.
3
Understand the author's counter-critique
The author says 'the inconsistency of this argument is clear.' This likely means Ibn Taymiyya's argument itself assumes a principle (that unproven premises cannot be foundational) which may not be self-evident or proven, leading to circularity or infinite regress.
The argument may be self-refuting: it claims no unproven premise can be foundational, but this claim itself is unproven.
4
Evaluate the exchange
Assess the strength of Ibn Taymiyya's critique and the author's objection. Does Ibn Taymiyya actually prove that logicians have no proof? Or does he merely assert? The author's remark suggests the critique may undercut itself.
This is a classic move in philosophical debate: turning the opponent's argument against them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الأولى : عدم بداهة هذا المقام السالب يستلزم وجود دليل لكي يكون صادقاً . ولكن ليس للمناطقة فيما يعتقد ابن تيمية ، دليل على هذا . فكيف إذن نتخذ قضية غير مبرهن عليها أساساً لعلم البرهان نفسه(٢) وتهافت هذه الحجة واضح .
The first argument: The non-self-evidence of this negative aspect necessitates the existence of a proof in order for it to be true. But, according to Ibn Taymiyya, logicians have no proof for this. So how then do we take an unproven proposition as a foundation for the science of demonstration itself? And the inconsistency of this argument is clear.
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn Taymiyya's first argument against the negative aspect of logic. The argument is that the foundational premise of Aristotelian logic — that definitions are necessary for conceptualization — is not self-evident. If it is not self-evident, it requires proof. Ibn Taymiyya claims logicians have no such proof. Therefore, it is absurd to base the entire science of demonstration (burhan) on an unproven proposition. The author of Manahij Bahth adds a brief critique: 'And the inconsistency of this argument is clear,' implying that the argument is self-defeating or flawed. The reference to the foundational premise being 'unproven' targets the logicians' assumption that the need for definition is axiomatic.

Ibn Taymiyya's Dilemma Against Definition
Ibn Taymiyya argues that definition leads to circularity or infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's dilemma against the necessity of definition and evaluate its logical force.

Core Idea
Definition either requires another definition (infinite regress) or is unnecessary (contradiction).

Ibn Taymiyya challenges the widely held view that definition (hadd) is necessary for conceptualization (tasawwur). He presents a crisp dilemma: either the thing defined is known through a definition or not. Both options lead to a problem. Let's walk through his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target claim
The claim under attack is: 'The desired concept cannot be attained except by definition.' This is the proposition Ibn Taymiyya seeks to refute.
2
Extract the two horns of the dilemma
Ibn Taymiyya splits the situation: either the thing defined is defined by a definition (hadd) or by something other than a definition (e.g., a known word or intuition).
This is a classic disjunctive syllogism structure.
3
Analyze the first horn: definition by definition
If the thing is defined by a definition, then that definition itself must be known. To know it, another definition is needed, leading to infinite regress (tasalsul) or circularity (dawr). Thus, the process never ends or loops back.
Infinite regress is impossible in a finite mind; circularity is logically vicious.
4
Analyze the second horn: definition without definition
If the thing is defined without a definition (e.g., by a known word or direct awareness), then the original claim that definition is necessary for conceptualization is false, because conceptualization occurred without it.
This horn shows the claim is self-defeating.
5
Synthesize the outcome
Both horns lead to a refutation of the necessity of definition. The dilemma is exhaustive and logically valid: if definition is needed, it fails; if not needed, the claim is false.
Ibn Taymiyya uses this to argue that definitions are not essential for knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : الحد هو القول الدال على حقيقة الحدود وماهيته ، فالحد إما أن يكون عرف المحدود بحد أو بغير حد . فإذا كان قد عرف المحدود بحد فان الأمر يدور ويتسلسل ، لأنه لا بد له من معرفة هذا الحد الأخير بحد آخر وإذا عرفه بغير حد ، كانت القضية - التصور المطلوب لا ينال إلا بالحد - قضية باطلة(١) .
The second argument: The definition is the statement indicating the reality and quiddity of the thing defined. So the definition either defines the thing defined by a definition or by something other than a definition. If it defines the thing defined by a definition, then the matter leads to circularity and infinite regress, because it is necessary to know this last definition by another definition. And if it defines it by something other than a definition, then the proposition — 'the desired concept cannot be attained except by definition' — is a false proposition.
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn Taymiyya's second argument against the necessity of definition (hadd). He first defines a definition as a statement that indicates the reality (haqiqah) and quiddity (mahiyyah) of the thing defined. He then poses a dilemma: either the thing defined is defined by a definition, or by something else. If by a definition, that definition itself must be known, requiring another definition, leading to infinite regress (tasalsul) or circularity (dawr). If by something else (like a known term), then the foundational claim that 'the desired concept cannot be attained except by definition' is false, since the thing was defined without a definition. In either case, the claim that definition is necessary for conceptualization fails.

Key Terms

دور
تسلسل

D) It becomes self-evident.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya's dilemma shows that the claim that definition is necessary for conceptualization is logically untenable because it either leads to an infinite regress or is self-defeating.

Ibn Taymiyya's Fourth Argument: No Perfect Definition Exists
Ibn Taymiyya argues every definition has been objected to, so definitions cannot yield certain knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's fourth argument against the possibility of perfect definitions and evaluate its logical strength.

Core Idea
If every definition has been objected to, definitions cannot yield certain knowledge.

Ibn Taymiyya continues his critique of Aristotelian logic by targeting definitions (hadd). He argues that no definition in any field has been universally accepted; even the most famous ones are contested. If conceptualization (tasawwur) through definitions is impossible, then certainty in knowledge is unattainable. This lesson walks you through his argument and its implications for Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conclusion
Read the passage and find the final claim: 'Therefore, we will not arrive at knowledge.' That is the conclusion Ibn Taymiyya aims to prove.
2
Find the evidence
He provides two examples: (1) the definition of man as 'rational animal' has famous objections, (2) Arab grammarians gave over twenty definitions of 'noun', all objected to. This is inductive evidence that no definition is immune.
3
Trace the epistemological chain
Ibn Taymiyya links definition to knowledge: conceptualization (tasawwur) is the basis of assent (tasdiq), and together they constitute knowledge. If conceptualization via definitions is impossible, then knowledge is impossible.
The chain: no correct definition → no correct tasawwur → no tasdiq → no knowledge.
4
Evaluate the argument
Ask: Does Ibn Taymiyya prove that no definition can ever be correct, or merely that no definition is universally accepted? This is a key distinction for assessing the argument's validity.
Consider whether the examples are representative and if the conclusion necessarily follows.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة : عدم استقراء أي حد من الحدود في أي فرع من فروع المعرفة الإنسانية . بل إن حد الإنسان المشهور - وهو الحيوان الناطق - عليه اعتراضات مشهورة . والنحاة العرب ذكروا للإسم على طريقة المنطق الأرسططاليسي الحقيقي أكثر من عشرين حداً أعترض عليها جميعاً . وعلى هذا الأساس لن نصل إلى تصور صحيح على الإطلاق . والتصور أساس التصديق والاثنان يكونان العلم . إذن فلن نصل إلى العلم (۳) .
The fourth argument: The non-existence of any definition in any branch of human knowledge that has been inductively verified. Rather, the famous definition of man — which is 'rational animal' — has famous objections against it. And Arab grammarians mentioned for the noun, according to the method of actual Aristotelian logic, more than twenty definitions, all of which were objected to. On this basis, we will never arrive at a correct conceptualization at all. And conceptualization is the basis of assent, and the two together constitute knowledge. Therefore, we will not arrive at knowledge.
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn Taymiyya's fourth argument, which challenges the very possibility of a perfect definition. He claims that no definition in any field has been verified by induction (i.e., universally accepted). As examples, he cites the classic definition of man as 'rational animal' (hayawan natiq), which has well-known objections. He also notes that Arab grammarians formulated over twenty definitions of the term 'noun' (ism) following Aristotelian method, all of which were rejected. Since conceptualization (tasawwur) is the basis of assent (tasdiq) and both together constitute knowledge ('ilm), and we cannot achieve correct conceptualization through definitions, it follows that we cannot attain knowledge through this method.

Ibn Taymiyya's Reductio on Aristotelian Definitions
Reductio argument against Aristotelian definitions as sole means to concepts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's reductio argument against Aristotelian definitions and evaluate its logical force.

Core Idea
If definitions were the only route to concepts, and definitions are impossible, we would have no concepts—but we do.

Ibn Taymiyya challenges the Aristotelian claim that real definitions (constituted by genus and differentia) are the only way to acquire concepts. He argues that such definitions are either impossible or extremely difficult to achieve. If that were the only path, we would have no concepts. Yet we observe concepts everywhere in the Islamic sciences. Therefore, the Aristotelian definition is not necessary for conceptualization.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target claim
The target is the Aristotelian doctrine that real definitions (al-ḥadd al-ḥaqīqī) composed of genus and differentia are the only means to acquire concepts.
This is the position of the falāsifa and some mutakallimūn.
2
Extract the impossibility premise
Ibn Taymiyya asserts that arriving at such a definition is either impossible (mutaʿadhdhir) or extremely difficult (mutaʿassir). Note that he does not prove impossibility here; he uses it as a conditional.
The argument works even if it is merely difficult, as long as definitions are not readily available.
3
Reconstruct the conditional argument
If definitions are impossible/difficult AND they are the only way to concepts, then we would never have concepts. This is a conditional: if P then Q. But we observe that we do have concepts (not Q). Therefore, by modus tollens, the antecedent is false: either definitions are not impossible or they are not the only way.
Ibn Taymiyya concludes that definitions are not necessary.
4
Observe the contradictory fact
We see concepts in all sciences (e.g., in fiqh, kalām, uṣūl). People understand terms like 'ṣalāh', 'ribā', 'ʿilla' without a full Aristotelian definition.
This is the empirical counterexample.
5
State the conclusion
Therefore, there is no need to rely on Aristotelian definitions to obtain concepts. Concepts can be acquired through other means, such as description (rasm), example, or customary usage.
Ibn Taymiyya does not deny definitions entirely; he denies their exclusivity and necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الخامسة : الحد الأرسططاليسي الحقيقي يتكون من الذاتيات المشتركة والمميزة ، أي من الجنس والفصل - والتوصل إلى هذا الحد إما متعذر وإما متعسر فلن نصل إذن إلى تصور من التصورات . ولكنا نرى في جميع نواحي العلوم تصورات قد وصلنا إليها . إذن لا حاجة للتوصل إلى التصور بواسطة الحد الأرسططاليسي (٤) .
The fifth argument: The real Aristotelian definition consists of the shared and distinguishing essential attributes, i.e., genus and differentia. And arriving at this definition is either impossible or extremely difficult. So we will not, then, arrive at any concept. But we see in all aspects of sciences concepts that we have arrived at. Therefore, there is no need to arrive at the concept by means of the Aristotelian definition.
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn Taymiyya's fifth argument, which focuses on the real Aristotelian definition composed of genus (jins) and differentia (fasl). He argues that attaining such a definition is either impossible (muta'adhdhir) or extremely difficult (muta'assir). If it is impossible, then we would never have any concepts, but we do have concepts in all sciences. If it is difficult, it is still not the only way. Since we clearly have concepts, the Aristotelian definition is not necessary for conceptualization. The argument is a reductio ad absurdum: if definitions were the sole means to concepts, and they are impossible to achieve, then we would have no concepts, which contradicts observable reality.

Ibn Taymiyya's Dilemma Against Definition
Ibn Taymiyya argues that definition either requires a prior definition or is unnecessary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's dilemma against definition and explain why it leads to circularity or infinite regress.

Core Idea
Ibn Taymiyya shows that the claim 'definition is necessary for concept attainment' is self-defeating.

Ibn Taymiyya challenges the foundational claim of Aristotelian logic that definition (hadd) is required to acquire concepts (tasawwur). He presents a devastating dilemma: either you already know the definiendum by a definition, which leads to infinite regress, or you know it without a definition, disproving the necessity. This lesson walks you through that logical trap.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target claim
Ibn Taymiyya is attacking the logicians' assertion that definition is necessary for attaining concepts. Find this claim in the passage.
The proposition: 'the required concept is only attained by definition'.
2
Extract the two horns of the dilemma
The passage presents two possibilities: either the definiendum is known by a definition, or by something else. Write them down.
3
Analyze the first horn: known by definition
If known by definition, that definition itself must be known. How do you know that definition? By another definition? This leads to either circularity or infinite regress.
4
Analyze the second horn: known without definition
If known without definition, then the claim that definition is necessary is false, since the definiendum was known without it.
5
Conclude the force of the dilemma
Either way, the logicians' position is untenable. Ibn Taymiyya uses this to argue that concepts are attained through other means.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : الحد هو القول الدال على حقيقة الحدود وماهيته ، فالحد إما أن يكون عرف المحدود بحد أو بغير حد . فإذا كان قد عرف المحدود بحد فان الأمر يدور ويتسلسل ، لأنه لا بد له من معرفة هذا الحد الأخير بحد آخر وإذا عرفه بغير حد ، كانت القضية - التصور المطلوب لا ينال إلا بالحد - قضية باطلة.
The second argument: The definition is the statement indicating the reality of the definiendum and its quiddity. Thus, the definition either the definiendum was known by a definition or by other than a definition. If it was known by a definition, then the matter involves circularity and infinite regress, because it is necessary to know that last definition by another definition. And if it was known by other than a definition, then the proposition — 'the required concept is only attained by definition' — is a false proposition.
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument against the necessity of definition for concept attainment. It begins by defining definition (hadd) as the statement that indicates the reality and quiddity of the thing defined (mahdud). Then it poses a dilemma: either the definiendum is known through a definition or through something else. If through a definition, then that definition itself would need another definition, leading to either circularity (dawr) or infinite regress (tasalsul), both unacceptable. If known without a definition, then the claim that 'the required concept is only attained by definition' becomes false, since the definiendum was actually known without a definition. Thus, the argument shows that the logicians' claim is self-defeating.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya's dilemma demonstrates that the necessity of definition for concept attainment is self-defeating, either leading to infinite regress or being falsified by knowledge without definition.

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of Definitional Certainty
Ibn Taymiyya argues that no definition is universally accepted.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's fourth argument against the necessity of definitions and evaluate its impact on the claim that definitions produce certain concepts.

Core Idea
If no definition survives objections, then definitions cannot be the foundation of certain knowledge.

Ibn Taymiyya here attacks the very possibility of providing a definition that is free from objections. He points to the famous definition of man as 'rational animal' and notes it is contested. He also cites the Arab grammarians who, following Aristotelian logic, proposed over twenty definitions of 'ism' (noun), all of which were criticized. From this he draws a devastating conclusion: if definitions were necessary for concepts, and concepts for assent, we would never have knowledge. Since we clearly do have knowledge, definitions cannot be necessary.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target of the critique
What claim is Ibn Taymiyya attacking? The claim that definitions (hadd) provide certain concepts (tasawwur), which are foundational for knowledge.
2
Examine the empirical evidence
What examples does he give to show that definitions are always contested? The definition of man as 'rational animal' and the over twenty definitions of 'ism' by Arab grammarians, all of which had objections.
3
Trace the logical chain
Ibn Taymiyya assumes a chain: definition → concept → assent → knowledge. If definitions are flawed, then concepts are flawed, then assent is flawed, then knowledge is impossible.
4
Assess the conclusion
Does the impossibility of perfect definitions actually prove that definitions are unnecessary for knowledge? Consider possible responses: maybe definitions are not the only path to concepts, or the objections are not fatal.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة : عدم استقراء أي حد من الحدود في أي فرع من فروع المعرفة الإنسانية . بل إن حد الإنسان المشهور - وهو الحيوان الناطق - عليه اعتراضات مشهورة . والنحاة العرب ذكروا للإسم على طريقة المنطق الأرسططاليسي الحقيقي أكثر من عشرين حداً أعترض عليها جميعاً . وعلى هذا الأساس لن نصل إلى تصور صحيح على الإطلاق . والتصور أساس التصديق والاثنان يكونان العلم . إذن فلن نصل إلى العلم.

On this basis, we will never attain a correct concept at all. And concept is the foundation of assent, and both constitute knowledge. Therefore, we will not attain knowledge.

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth argument, which points to the historical failure to produce a universally accepted definition in any discipline. Ibn Taymiyya uses the famous definition of man as 'rational animal' (al-hayawan al-natiq) and notes that even this has well-known objections. He further cites the Arab grammarians who proposed over twenty definitions of 'ism' (noun) following the Aristotelian method, all of which were criticized. Therefore, he concludes that if definitions are necessary for concepts, and no definition is sound, then no correct concept can be attained. Since concept (tasawwur) is the foundation of assent (tasdiq), and both are needed for knowledge ('ilm), we would be left without any knowledge. But we clearly have knowledge, so definition cannot be necessary.

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of Genus-Differentia Definition
Ibn Taymiyya critiques the necessity and attainability of the Aristotelian definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's fifth argument against the Aristotelian definition and evaluate its logical force.

Core Idea
If a definition method is impractical, it cannot be a universal requirement for knowledge.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya targets the very foundation of Aristotelian logic — the claim that genuine concepts (tasawwurat) must be obtained through definitions composed of genus and differentia. He argues that this method is either impossible or extremely difficult, yet we clearly possess many concepts in all areas of sciences. Therefore, the Aristotelian definition is dispensable for attaining concepts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target doctrine
The Aristotelian true definition (al-hadd al-haqiqi) consists of the common essential properties (genus) and the distinguishing essential property (differentia). This is what Ibn Taymiyya challenges.
2
Extract the premise about difficulty
Ibn Taymiyya states that attaining such a definition is either impossible (mut'adhdhir) or extremely difficult (mut'asir). Notice the disjunction — he allows that it might be merely difficult, not outright impossible.
3
Observe the empirical evidence
He points to the fact that we already have many concepts in all sciences (e.g., in fiqh, kalam, grammar) without having gone through such a definitional process.
This is a key empirical premise: concepts exist without genus-differentia definitions.
4
Reconstruct the logical conclusion
If the Aristotelian definition were necessary for concept attainment, then we would not have concepts where that definition is unattainable. But we do have concepts. Therefore, the Aristotelian definition is not necessary. This is a modus tollens argument.
The argument is valid in form: If P then Q; not Q; therefore not P.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الخامسة : الحد الأرسططاليسي الحقيقي يتكون من الذاتيات المشتركة والمميزة ، أي من الجنس والفصل - والتوصل إلى هذا الحد إما متعذر وإما متعسر فلن نصل إذن إلى تصور من التصورات . ولكنا نرى في جميع نواحي العلوم تصورات قد وصلنا إليها . إذن لا حاجة للتوصل إلى التصور بواسطة الحد الأرسططاليسي.
The fifth argument: The true Aristotelian definition consists of the common and distinguishing essential properties, i.e., genus and differentia. And attaining this definition is either impossible or difficult. Therefore, we will not attain any concept from among concepts. But we see in all areas of sciences concepts that we have attained. Hence, there is no need to attain concept by means of the Aristotelian definition.
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth argument, focusing on the structure of the Aristotelian definition. Ibn Taymiyya points out that the true definition (al-hadd al-haqiqi) must include the common essential properties (al-dhatiyyat al-mushtaraka), i.e., genus (jins), and the distinguishing property (fasl). Attaining such a definition is either impossible (mut'adhdhir) or extremely difficult (mut'asir). Since we clearly possess many concepts in all fields of knowledge without such definitions, the Aristotelian definition is unnecessary. The argument implies that if the method for obtaining concepts through definition is flawed or inaccessible, then it cannot be a requirement for knowledge.

Ibn Taymiyya's Sixth Argument: Simple Realities and Definition
Ibn Taymiyya argues that simple realities cannot be defined yet are known, so definition is not necessary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's sixth argument against the necessity of definition for concept attainment and evaluate its strength.

Core Idea
Simple realities have no definition, yet we know them — so definitions are not essential.

Ibn Taymiyya presents his sixth argument to challenge the Aristotelian claim that definitions are necessary for concepts. He points to simple realities like the intellect, which cannot be defined because they have no parts or genus, yet they are known. This suggests that concepts can be formed without definitions. In this lesson, we will dissect his argument and see how it undermines the logical requirement for a complete definition (hadd haqiqi).

Guided Path
1
Identify the target claim
What Aristotelian position is Ibn Taymiyya attacking? Find the claim that a complete definition (hadd haqiqi) is necessary for concept formation.
2
Extract the key premise about simple realities
What are simple realities (al-haqa'iq al-basita)? According to the passage, they have no composition and do not fall under a genus. Therefore, they cannot be defined.
3
Find the counterexample
What example does Ibn Taymiyya give? He cites the intellect ('aql). Despite being indefinable, the Aristotelians have a concept of it. This shows that concepts exist without definitions.
4
Assess the logical implication
If simple realities are known without definition, what follows for composite species? Ibn Taymiyya argues that knowing species is even easier because their genera are known and individuals are famous. Therefore, definitions are not necessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السادسة . وهذه الحجة ذات فروع ثلاثة . . . إن الحقائق التي لا تركيب فيها والتي لا تندرج تحت جنس ـ أي الحقائق البسيطة ـ لا حد لها . فالعقل وهو من تلك الحقائق التي لا تركيب فيها لا يحد . ولكننا نجد لدى الأرسططاليسيين تصورات للعقل والحقائق لا تركيب فيها . وهذا يثبت أنه يمكن التوصل إلى التصور بغير الحد . . . وإذا أمكن معرفة التصورات التي لا تركيب فيها بدون الحد ، كانت معرفة التصورات ـ أي الأنواع ـ أسهل لمعرفة أجناسها ولشهرة أشخاصها . . . وليس من الضروري في منطق أرسطو ، أن يتوقف التصديق على التصور التام ـ أي تصور الذات ، أو الماهية ، بل يكفي أن تتوصل إلى الخاصة . ونفس العلم لا يمكن التوصل إليه إلا بهذا الطريق . هذا اعتراف واضح بأن التصور لا يتوقف على الحد الحقيقي.
The sixth argument. And this argument has three branches... Indeed, the realities that have no composition and that do not fall under a genus — that is, the simple realities — have no definition. For the intellect, which is among those realities that have no composition, is not defined. But we find among the Aristotelians concepts of the intellect and realities that have no composition. And this proves that it is possible to attain the concept without definition. And if it is possible to know the concepts that have no composition without definition, then knowing the concepts — i.e., the species — is easier because of knowing their genera and the fame of their individuals. And it is not necessary in Aristotle's logic for assent to depend on the complete concept — that is, the concept of the essence or quiddity — rather, it suffices to arrive at the property. And knowledge itself cannot be attained except by this path. This is a clear admission that the concept does not depend on the real definition.
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth argument, which has three branches. Ibn Taymiyya focuses on simple realities (al-haqa'iq al-basita) that have no composition and do not fall under a genus. For example, the intellect ('aql) is such a simple reality and cannot be defined. Yet the Aristotelians themselves have concepts of the intellect and other simple realities. This proves that concepts can be obtained without definitions. Furthermore, if simple uncomposed things can be known without definition, then composite species are even easier to know because their genera are known and their individuals are well-known. Ibn Taymiyya also notes that, according to Aristotle, assent (tasdiq) does not require a complete definition (tasawwur tamm) of the essence; it suffices to know the distinguishing property (khassa). He interprets this as an admission that true definition is not necessary for concept attainment.

Key Terms
الحقائق البسيطة
العقل
الخاصة

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya uses the indefinability of simple realities to argue that definitions are not indispensable for concept formation, challenging a core assumption of Aristotelian logic.

Seventh Argument: Definitions Presuppose Prior Knowledge
Ibn Taymiyya critiques definitional knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's epistemological objection to definitions and explain why definitions cannot convey new concepts.

Core Idea
A definition cannot teach a new concept because you must already know the words.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya presents a powerful critique of the logical claim that definitions produce new concepts. He argues that to understand a definition, you must already understand the meanings of its individual words, and that understanding presupposes prior conceptual knowledge. This challenges the very foundation of definitional reasoning in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Understand the claim being critiqued
Identify what logicians claim about definitions: that they can convey new concepts (tasawwurat) to the listener.
2
Identify the two conditions
Note the two things the listener must have to understand a definition: understanding of the individual words (al-mufradat) and their indication (dalala) of their meanings.
3
Unpack the presupposition
Recognize that knowing a word's signification is preceded by a concept (tasawwur) of the meaning. Without that prior concept, the listener cannot connect the word to its meaning.
4
Apply the dilemma
If the listener already has the concept, the definition is redundant. If he does not, he cannot understand the definition. Either way, the definition fails to produce a new concept.
5
Evaluate the conclusion
Assess the implication: definitions cannot serve as a primary source of new conceptual knowledge, contrary to the logicians' claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السابعة : لا يمكن للسامع فهم الحد ، إلا إذا فهمت مفردات ألفاظه ودلالتها على معانيها . والعلم بدلالة اللفظ على المعنى الموضوع له ، مسبوق بتصور المعنى . فإن كان السامع قد تصور المعنى قبل سماعه . لم تعد هناك ثمة حاجة إلى القول بأنه تصوره حين سماعه.
The seventh argument: It is not possible for the listener to understand the definition unless he understands the individual words and their indication of their meanings. And knowledge of the word's indication of its assigned meaning is preceded by a concept of the meaning. If the listener has already conceptualized the meaning before hearing it, then there is no longer any need to say that he conceptualized it at the time of hearing.
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh argument, which focuses on the epistemological role of language in definitions. Ibn Taymiyya points out that to understand a definition, the listener must first comprehend the individual words used in it and how they point to their meanings. However, knowing the word's signification (dalala) presupposes that one already has a concept (tasawwur) of that meaning. Therefore, if the listener already possesses the concept before hearing the definition, the definition is unnecessary. If he does not, then the definition cannot convey the concept because he cannot understand the words. Thus, the definition fails to produce a new concept, contrary to the logicians' claim.

Key Takeaway
Definitions cannot convey truly new concepts because they rely on words whose meanings must already be known.

Ibn Taymiyya on the Independence of Concepts from Definitions
Ibn Taymiyya argues that definition as verbal speech is unnecessary because concepts are mental and independent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain Ibn Taymiyya's eighth argument against the necessity of verbal definitions for concept formation.

Core Idea
Concepts are mental, not linguistic; definitions merely articulate them.

Ibn Taymiyya continues his critique of the logicians' claim that definitions are essential for acquiring concepts. In this eighth argument, he emphasizes that the mental conceptualization of meanings does not depend on words. The speaker and listener can both form concepts without uttering or hearing a definition. Understanding this argument helps you see the epistemological independence of mental understanding from verbal expression.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target claim
The logicians claim that definitions (ḥudūd) are necessary for attaining concepts (taṣawwurāt). Ibn Ṭaymiyya attacks this by analyzing the nature of definitions.
2
Restate Ibn Taymiyya's argument
If a definition is merely the speech of the definer (qaul al-ḥādd), then concepts are mental (dhihnī) and do not require uttered words. The speaker already has the concept before speaking, and the listener can form the concept without hearing the definer.
This is a reductio against the necessity of verbal definitions.
3
Evaluate the premises
Consider whether concepts can truly exist independently of language. Think of a meaning you understand without having heard a formal definition—like the meaning of 'thirst' or 'justice'.
Ibn Ṭaymiyya's argument relies on introspection.
4
Apply to an Islamic science example
In Uṣūl al-Fiqh, the concept of 'illa (effective cause) is grasped by mujtahids through mental reasoning (taṣawwur) without always needing an explicit definition. This shows the practical relevance of Ibn Ṭaymiyya's critique.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثامنة : وهذه الحجة تكاد تكون ترديداً لما قبلها . . إذا كان الحد قول الحاد ، فان تصور المعاني شيء ذهني لا يفتقر إلى الألفاظ . فالمتكلم يتصور معنى ما يقوله بدون لفظ ، كما أن السامع يمكنه ذلك بدون أن يخاطبه المتكلم.
The eighth argument: And this argument is almost a repetition of the previous one. If the definition is the statement of the definer, then the concept of meanings is something mental that does not require words. For the speaker conceptualizes the meaning of what he says without a word, just as the listener can do that without the speaker addressing him.
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage presents the eighth argument, which Ibn Taymiyya notes is almost a repetition of the seventh. He reiterates that definitions are verbal statements (qawl) produced by the definer. However, concepts (tasawwur) are mental entities that do not depend on words. The speaker himself has a concept of what he intends before uttering the words, and the listener can form concepts independently without being addressed. This reinforces the idea that verbal definitions are not essential for concept attainment; concepts can be acquired through direct experience or other means.

Key Takeaway
The eighth argument reinforces that verbal definitions are not necessary for conceptual understanding; concepts exist independently in the mind.
