Ibn Taymiyya's Tenth Argument: Refutation Implies Prior Conception
Ibn Taymiyya's tenth argument against definition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's tenth argument against definition and explain why the possibility of refutation (naqd/mu'arada) implies that the defined is already conceived.

Core Idea
If you can refute a definition, you already know the thing defined.

Ibn Taymiyya's tenth argument challenges the very purpose of definition (hadd) in Islamic logic. He observes that logicians accept that definitions can be invalidated through naqd (breaking the universal) or mu'arada (counterexample). But this invalidation only makes sense if the defined term is already known. Let's walk through this argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two methods of invalidation
The passage lists two ways an objector can invalidate a definition: naqd (refutation by showing the definition's universal term fails to cover all instances) and mu'arada (counterargument by offering an alternative definition or counterexample).
These are standard tools in jadal (disputation) for testing definitions.
2
Recognize the presupposition of refutation
Ibn Taymiyya argues that for any refutation (naqd or mu'arada) to be directed at a definition, the objector must already have a conception of the defined thing. Otherwise, how would they know the definition is incorrect?
This is the key logical move: refutation presupposes knowledge of the definiendum.
3
Draw the conclusion: definition is unnecessary for conception
If the defined can be conceived without the definition (since it is needed to refute the definition), then the definition is not the source of conception. Hence definitions are not necessary for acquiring conceptual knowledge.
This does not deny all value of definitions, only their epistemic primacy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة العاشرة : يمكن للمعترض على الحد أن يبطله . وهذا الإبطال يحدث عن طريقين : الطريق الأول النقض في الطرد تارة ، والمعارضة طوراً . ولا يتوجه النقض ولا المعارضة على الحد إلا إذا تصور المحدود . فأمكننا بذلك تصور المحدود بدون الحد .
الحجة الحادية عشر : نسبية البداهة والنظر في التصورات. من التصورات ما يكون بديهياً لا يحتاج إلى حد . وكون العلم بديهياً أو نظرياً شيء نسبي ، فالنظري عند شخص قد يكون بديهياً عند الآخر . فلا حاجة إذن إلى التوصل بالحد إلى هذه التصورات .
ونسبية كل شيء . . . قال بها بروتاغوراس السوفسطائي حين أراد أن ينقد أصول المعرفة « الإنسان هو مقياس وجود ما يوجد منها ومقياس لا وجود ما لا يوجد » ثم أخذ بها الشكاك بعد ، فطبقوها على الحد ، كما طبقوها على نواحي العلم كله . فلم تعد حقيقة من حقائق العلم ثابتة أو مستقرة ، بل كل شيء ـ كما يقول هر قليطس ـ في تغير مستمر .
The tenth argument: The objector to the definition can invalidate it. This invalidation occurs through two ways: the first way is refutation (naqd) of the universal (tard) sometimes, and counterargument (mu'arada) at other times. Refutation and counterargument are only directed against the definition if the defined thing is conceived. Thus we are able to conceive the defined thing without the definition.
The eleventh argument: The relativity of intuition (badaha) and theory (nazar) in conceptions. Some conceptions are intuitive and do not need a definition. The fact that knowledge is intuitive or theoretical is relative; what is theoretical for one person may be intuitive for another. Therefore there is no need to arrive at these conceptions through definition.
And the relativity of everything... Protagoras the Sophist advocated it when he wanted to criticize the foundations of knowledge: 'Man is the measure of the existence of what exists and the measure of the non-existence of what does not exist.' Then the skeptics later adopted it and applied it to definition, just as they applied it to all aspects of knowledge. So no truth of knowledge remains fixed or stable; rather everything – as Heraclitus says – is in constant change.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage presents two of Ibn Taymiyya's arguments against the claim that definition (hadd) produces knowledge of the defined. The first argument (tenth) focuses on the logicians' own admission that definitions can be refuted by naqd (breaking the universal) or mu'arada (counterexample). If a definition is open to refutation, then the defined must already be known in some sense, otherwise the refutation could not be applied. Thus the definition is unnecessary for conception. The second argument (eleventh) introduces relativity: what is intuitive (badihi) for one person is theoretical (nadhari) for another. Since every conception is either intuitive or derived from intuition, and what is intuitive needs no definition, definitions are dispensable. The text then links this to Protagoras' 'man is the measure' doctrine and Heraclitus' flux, suggesting that the skeptics used such relativism to undermine all stable knowledge. These arguments challenge the foundational role of definition in the logical tradition, echoing earlier Greek skeptical tropes.

Key Terms
النقض (al-naqd): Refutation of a definition by showing that its universal term does not apply to all members of the defined class (naqd al-tard). [Note: In later jadal texts, naqd refers specifically to breaking the 'tard' condition of a definition.]
المعارضة (al-mu'arada): Counterargument against a definition by providing an alternative definition or counterexample. [Note: In syllogistic contexts, mu'arada can mean a parallel argument, not just against definitions.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the tenth argument, what is the consequence of the possibility of refuting a definition?
A) Definitions are always false.
B) The defined must already be conceived without the definition.
C) Definitions are only useful for intuitive concepts.
D) Definitions must be proven by a syllogism.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya's tenth argument exposes a circularity: to refute a definition, you need prior conception, so definitions are not foundational for conception.

?
Using the fiqh example of 'ghasb' (unlawful taking), demonstrate how a mu'arada could be offered by providing a counterexample, and show that this only works if the opponent already has a conception of ghasb.

Ibn Taymiyya's Eleventh Argument: Relativity of Intuition and Theory
Ibn Taymiyya's eleventh argument against definition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain Ibn Taymiyya's relativity argument against definition and identify the skeptical implications he warns against.

Core Idea
What is intuitive for one is theoretical for another – definitions become redundant.

Ibn Taymiyya's eleventh argument attacks definitions from another angle: the distinction between intuitive (badihi) and theoretical (nadhari) knowledge is relative. What requires reasoning for one person may be self-evident for another. Consequently, definitions are not universally necessary. He then warns that this relativity, if taken to extremes, leads to Protagorean skepticism and Heraclitean flux.

Guided Path
1
Understand the relativity claim
The passage states that some conceptions are intuitive (badihi) and need no definition. But whether a conception is intuitive or theoretical varies from person to person. Something that requires reasoning for you may be self-evident for someone else.
This is a direct challenge to the logicians' universal claim that all conceptions require definition or derivation from intuition.
2
Apply the relativity to definitions
If a concept is already intuitive for someone, a definition is unnecessary for that person. And if it is theoretical, it is derived from intuitive premises, not from definitions. Therefore definitions are never essential for conception.
Ibn Taymiyya here does not deny that definitions can be useful for clarification, just that they are necessary.
3
Identify the skeptical link
Ibn Taymiyya connects the relativity argument to the broader skepticism of Protagoras ('man is the measure') and Heraclitus (constant flux). He warns that if we accept total relativity, no stable knowledge remains.
This is a caution: while Ibn Taymiyya himself uses relativity to undermine definitions, he does not endorse full-blown skepticism.

Source Text

Key Terms
البداهة (al-badaha): Intuitive knowledge that does not require a definition or proof; self-evident conceptions. [Note: Term stable across the tradition.]
النظر (al-nazar): Theoretical knowledge acquired through reasoning, as opposed to intuition. [Note: Stable; in kalam, nazar specifically means speculative reasoning.]

Ibn Taymiyya's First Argument: Definition as Unproven Claim
Ibn Taymiyya's first argument against definition as knowledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's first argument against definition as a source of knowledge and evaluate its epistemological force.

Core Idea
Definitions are unproven claims, so they cannot transmit certain knowledge.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya launches a critique of the logicians' claim that definitions (hudud) provide conceptual knowledge. He presents a dilemma: either the hearer already knows the defined thing, making the definition redundant, or he does not, in which case an unproven assertion cannot give knowledge. This argument challenges the very foundation of the definitional enterprise. By working through this argument, you will learn to identify a classic skeptical challenge to definitions and assess its validity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' claim
Logicians assert that a definition (al-hadd), composed of genus and differentia, gives conceptual knowledge (tasawwur) of the defined thing. Find where this claim is assumed in the argument.
The passage refers to 'al-muqām al-mūjib' (the positive position) that definitions yield knowledge.
2
Find the condition placed on definitions
The passage states that logicians stipulate that definitions must not be proven by demonstration (burhan). This is to avoid circularity or infinite regress. Locate the Arabic phrase 'يشترط ألا يكون الحد مبرهناً عليه'.
This condition is key to the argument: definitions are thus unsupported.
3
Understand Ibn Taymiyya's dilemma
He presents two cases: the addressee either knows the defined thing already (without the definition) or does not. In the first case, the definition is redundant. In the second, the learner relies on an unproven claim, which cannot produce knowledge.
The dilemma is exhaustive: there is no third option.
4
Trace the conclusion
From the dilemma, Ibn Taymiyya concludes that on both suppositions, the definition fails to convey knowledge. Therefore, definitions are epistemically useless for acquiring new conceptual knowledge.
Notice the structure: a dilemma leading to a universal negative conclusion.
5
Evaluate the argument
Consider whether the argument assumes that knowledge must come from demonstration. Could a definition be known through intuition or common sense? Does the argument prove that definitions are false, or only that they are not proven?
This step encourages critical engagement with Ibn Taymiyya's reasoning.

نقد المقام الموجب : وينقسم هذا النقد إلى قسمين - قسم يتمثل في صورة حجج يوجهها ابن تيمية إلى هذا المقام - وقسم ينقد به ابن تيمية عناصر الحد وفي هذا القسم الأخير يظهر ابتكاره ظهوراً تاماً على عكس الأول ، إذ يبدو فيه عمله الإبتكاري ضئيلاً .
أما القسم الأول - فيتلخص في الحجج الآتية :
الحجة الأولى : إن الحد هو قول الحاد ودعواه ، ولكن يشترط ألا يكون الحد مبرهناً عليه ، فهو إذن لا دليل عليه ولا حجة . فالمخاطب إما أن يعلم المحدود بدون قول الحاد أو بقوله . ففي الحالة الأولى لا يكون قد توصل إلى المحدود بالحد وفي الحالة الثانية لا يكون قول الحد - وهو لا دليل عليه ولا برهان - أفاد العلم بشيء . وعلى التقديرين لا يفيد الحد معرفة المحدود(۱) .
Critique of the positive position: This critique is divided into two parts – a part consisting of arguments that Ibn Taymiyya directs against this position – and a part in which Ibn Taymiyya criticizes the elements of definition. In this latter part his innovation appears fully, unlike the first, in which his innovative work appears minimal. As for the first part, it is summarized in the following arguments: The first argument: The definition is the statement and claim of the definer, but it is stipulated that the definition not be proven by demonstration (burhan). Thus it has no proof and no argument. The addressee either knows the defined thing without the definer's statement or by it. In the first case, he has not arrived at the defined thing through the definition. In the second case, the statement of the definer – which has no proof and no demonstration – does not provide knowledge of anything. On both suppositions, the definition does not convey knowledge of the defined.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage introduces the overall structure of Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the positive position (that definition yields knowledge) and presents the first specific argument. The critique is divided into two parts: one that advances arguments against the utility of definitions, and another that critically examines the elements of which a definition is composed (genus, differentia, etc.). The author notes that the second part is more original. The first argument runs as follows: A definition is merely a claim (da'wa) by the definer; moreover, logicians stipulate that a definition must not itself be proven by demonstration (burhan), because that would lead to circularity or infinite regress. Therefore, the definition is unsubstantiated. The person receiving the definition either already knows the thing defined without it (so the definition is redundant) or does not know it. In the latter case, he relies on an unproven claim, which cannot produce certain knowledge. In either scenario, the definition fails to give knowledge of the defined. This is a classic skeptical argument against definition as a source of conception.

قول الحاد
دعوى
مبرهناً عليه

According to the first argument, what is the condition placed on definitions by logicians that undermines their epistemic value?

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya's first argument exposes the epistemic weakness of definitions by showing they are either superfluous or unsupported claims, thus they cannot convey certain knowledge.

Ibn Taymiyya's First Argument: The Dilemma of Prior Knowledge
First argument against definition producing knowledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's first argument against definitions as a dilemma and evaluate its force.

Core Idea
Definitions cannot produce knowledge: either you already know the defined, or you cannot trust an unproven claim.

Ibn Taymiyya begins his critique with a simple yet powerful logical dilemma. If a definition is presented to someone who already knows the defined thing, the definition is superfluous. If presented to someone who does not know it, the definition is just an unsupported claim that cannot be verified. In this lesson, we will unpack this argument and see how it targets the very foundation of the logicians' project.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
In the first argument, Ibn Taymiyya presents two possibilities: either the listener knows the defined before the definition or he does not. Find these in the text.
2
Examine the consequence for each horn
If the listener already knows, the definition adds nothing. If he does not know, the definition is an unproven statement. Why does Ibn Taymiyya say it is unproven?
3
Connect to the condition of non-demonstration
Ibn Taymiyya notes that logicians stipulate that definitions are not themselves proven by demonstration. Why does this weaken the definition's epistemic status?
4
Formalize the argument as a dilemma
Write the argument in the form: Either P or Q; if P then R; if Q then S; therefore, in either case, definition fails to produce knowledge.

نقد المقام الموجب : وينقسم هذا النقد إلى قسمين - قسم يتمثل في صورة حجج يوجهها ابن تيمية إلى هذا المقام - وقسم ينقد به ابن تيمية عناصر الحد وفي هذا القسم الأخير يظهر ابتكاره ظهوراً تاماً على عكس الأول ، إذ يبدو فيه عمله الإبتكاري ضئيلاً . أما القسم الأول - فيتلخص في الحجج الآتية : الحجة الأولى : إن الحد هو قول الحاد ودعواه ، ولكن يشترط ألا يكون الحد مبرهناً عليه ، فهو إذن لا دليل عليه ولا حجة . فالمخاطب إما أن يعلم المحدود بدون قول الحاد أو بقوله . ففي الحالة الأولى لا يكون قد توصل إلى المحدود بالحد وفي الحالة الثانية لا يكون قول الحد - وهو لا دليل عليه ولا برهان - أفاد العلم بشيء . وعلى التقديرين لا يفيد الحد معرفة المحدود . الحجة الثانية : الحد لا يمنع ولا يقام عليه دليل ، بل يبطل بالنقض والمعارضة هذا ما يقرره المناطقة . وهو دليل على أن الحد لا يفيد التصور ، لأنه إذا كان الحاد لا يثبت صحة الحد وصدقه ، كان من الممتنع أن يعرف المخاطب المحدود بالحد لأن قوله يصبح متردداً أو محتملاً للصدق وللكذب ، فتصور المحدود بالحد لا يمكن بدون صدق الحاد . ولا نستطيع أن نعلم صدق الحاد بمجرد الحد لاشتراط عدم البرهنة عليه . إذن فالحد لا يصل بنا إلى المحدود . ولكن المناطقة « يعظمون أمر هذه الحدود » ويرون أنها هي وحدها التي تصل بهم إلى « الكنه » أو « الماهية » أو « الحقيقة » أو بمعنى أدق هي الغاية من اليقين العقلي . ويقيمون العلم كله على أساس الحد . والحد خبر واحد عن أمر عقلي لا حسي ، يحتمل الصدق والكذب وفي الآن عينه لا يقبلون « خبر الواحد » ويرون أنه لا يصل بنا إلى العلم القطعي ، بينما لدى ناقل هذا الخبر من الأدلة الحسية ما يفيد اليقين . فنقل خبر الواحد هو نوع من نقل الحد بل يمتاز عنه أيضاً كما رأينا . إذ أنه يستند على التصور الحسي ، بينما الحد يستند على التصور العقلي . فتفضيل أحدهما على الآخر يعود إلى مجرد تحكم وتعصب في الوضع . الحجة الثالثة : إذا كان الحد هو الطريق إلى تصور المحدود ، فلا يمكن ذلك إلا بعد أن نعلم صحة الحد ، ولا يمكن معرفة صحة الحد قبل تصور المحدود والمحدود لا يمكن التوصل إليه إلا بالحد ، فامتنعت معرفة صحة الحد . الحجة الرابعة : التوصل إلى الحدود بالصفات الذاتية والعرضية . فان لم يعلم الإنسان أن المحدود موصوف بهذه الصفات ، امتنع التصور . لأن من البديهي أن الإنسان لا يتصور ما لا يعلم ، وإن علم أنه موصوف به لم تكن ثمة حاجة إلى الحد . وفي كلتا الحالتين لا يفيد الحد التصور . إننا إذا قلنا « حيوان ناطق » ولم نعلم أنه حد الإنسان ، وجب علينا أولاً أن نعلم النسبة بين الاثنين - بين الحيوانية والناطقية - فاذا لم نكن قد تصورنا الحيوان الناطق ، كان لا بد أولاً أن نتصوره . . . ثم أن نعرف ثانياً النسبة بينه وبين الإنسان .
Critique of the positive position: This critique is divided into two parts—a part consisting of arguments that Ibn Taymiyya directs against this position, and a part in which Ibn Taymiyya critiques the elements of definition. In this latter part his innovation appears fully, contrary to the first, where his innovative work seems minimal. As for the first part, it is summarized in the following arguments. The first argument: The definition is the statement and claim of the definer, but it is stipulated that the definition not be proven; therefore it has no evidence and no proof. The addressee either knows the defined without the definer's statement or by his statement. In the first case, he has not arrived at the defined through the definition; in the second case, the statement of definition—having no evidence or proof—does not convey knowledge of anything. In both cases, the definition does not give knowledge of the defined. The second argument: The definition is not immune and no proof is established for it; rather it is invalidated by counterexample and objection. This is what the logicians affirm. This is evidence that the definition does not produce conceptualization, because if the definer does not establish the correctness and truth of the definition, it is impossible for the addressee to know the defined through the definition, since his statement becomes dubious or subject to truth and falsehood. So conceptualization of the defined by the definition cannot occur without the truth of the definer. And we cannot know the truth of the definer merely by the definition due to the condition that it not be proven. Therefore the definition does not lead us to the defined. But the logicians 'magnify the matter of these definitions' and see that they alone lead to 'the essence' or 'the quiddity' or 'the reality'; or more precisely, they are the goal of intellectual certainty. They build all knowledge on the basis of definition. And the definition is a single report about an intellectual matter, not a sensory one, subject to truth and falsehood. Yet at the same time they do not accept 'the single report' and see that it does not lead us to certain knowledge, while the transmitter of this report has sensory evidence that yields certainty. For transmitting a single report is a type of transmitting a definition, but it also excels over it as we have seen. Because it relies on sensory conceptualization, while the definition relies on intellectual conceptualization. So preferring one over the other amounts to mere willfulness and bias in convention. The third argument: If the definition is the path to conceptualizing the defined, then that is only possible after we know the correctness of the definition; and it is impossible to know the correctness of the definition before conceptualizing the defined—and the defined cannot be reached except by the definition—so knowledge of the correctness of the definition is impossible. The fourth argument: Attaining definitions is through essential and accidental attributes. If a person does not know that the defined is described by these attributes, conceptualization is impossible, because it is self-evident that one cannot conceptualize what one does not know; and if he knows that it is described by them, then there is no need for the definition. In both cases, the definition does not produce conceptualization. If we say 'rational animal' and we do not know that it is the definition of human, we must first know the relation between the two—between animality and rationality—so if we have not conceptualized 'rational animal', we must first conceptualize it... then we must secondarily know the relation between it and human.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage is a critical exposition of Ibn Taymiyya's arguments against the logicians' claim that definitions (hudud) produce conceptual knowledge (tasawwur). The author first divides the critique into two parts: one comprising arguments against the positive position, and another critiquing the elements of definition itself, where Ibn Taymiyya's originality is said to be more apparent. The passage then presents four specific arguments. 

The first argument is based on the nature of definition as an unproven statement. If the listener already knows the defined, the definition is superfluous; if he does not, the definition cannot provide knowledge because it has no proof to establish its truth. 

The second argument points out that logicians themselves admit definitions are not immune to refutation by counterexample or objection. Since the truth of the definition cannot be independently established, the listener cannot be certain that the definition corresponds to reality, and thus it fails to produce certain conceptualization. The author further notes the inconsistency of logicians who accept definitions as certain but reject single-reports (khabar al-wahid) even though the latter often have sensory backing. 

The third argument presents a circularity: to know the definition is correct, one must already conceptualize the defined, but the definition is supposed to be the means to that conceptualization. This creates a vicious circle. 

The fourth argument deals with the attributes used in definitions. If the listener does not know the attributes, he cannot grasp the definition; if he knows them, he already has conceptual knowledge. The example of 'rational animal' as the definition of human illustrates that one would need prior understanding of the relationship between the parts and the whole. 

All four arguments collectively aim to show that definitions, as conceived by logicians, are incapable of yielding new conceptual knowledge. The commentary should note that Ibn Taymiyya's critique is epistemological, not merely logical, and it challenges the foundational role of definitions in the Aristotelian tradition.

النقض والمعارضة

Key Takeaway

Ibn Taymiyya's Second Argument: Inconsistency with Khabar al-Wahid
Second argument: inconsistency with khabar al-wahid

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify Ibn Taymiyya's second argument that definitions are no more reliable than single-reports (khabar al-wahid) and evaluate the inconsistency he exposes in logicians' epistemology.

Core Idea
Logicians reject single-reports but accept definitions, yet definitions are even less certain than sensory reports.

Ibn Taymiyya's second argument strikes at the heart of the logicians' epistemological double standard. They dismiss single-reports (khabar al-wahid) as incapable of yielding certainty, yet they champion definitions that are, in fact, similar in nature but lack the sensory grounding of those reports. In this lesson, we will explore this argument and its implications for the theory of knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Understand the logicians' stance on definitions
Logicians claim that definitions are certain and lead to knowledge of essences. They 'magnify the matter of definitions' and base all knowledge on them.
2
Understand the status of khabar al-wahid
A single-report (khabar al-wahid) is a report from one transmitter about a sensory matter. Logicians reject it as yielding certainty. Why?
3
Compare the epistemic status of definitions and khabar al-wahid
Both are single reports: a definition is a single report about an intellectual matter, while khabar al-wahid is about a sensory matter. But the latter has sensory evidence backing it, while the definition does not. So definitions are less certain.
4
Expose the inconsistency
Ibn Taymiyya argues that preferring definitions over khabar al-wahid is arbitrary and biased. If logicians were consistent, they would either accept both or reject both. Their actual position is a double standard.

Commentary
This passage is a critical exposition of Ibn Taymiyya's arguments against the logicians' claim that definitions (hudud) produce conceptual knowledge (tasawwur). The author further notes the inconsistency of logicians who accept definitions as certain but reject single-reports (khabar al-wahid) even though the latter often have sensory backing. 

The third argument presents a circularity: to know the definition is correct, one must already conceptualize the defined, but the definition is supposed to be the means to that conceptualization. This creates a vicious circle.

According to Ibn Taymiyya's second argument, why is the logicians' rejection of khabar al-wahid inconsistent with their acceptance of definitions?
A) Because definitions are longer than khabar al-wahid.

Ibn Taymiyya's Third Argument: The Circularity of Definition
Third argument: circularity of definition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's third argument that definitions involve a vicious circle and explain why it undermines the claim that definitions produce conceptual knowledge.

Core Idea
To verify a definition, you must already know the defined; but that knowledge is supposed to come from the definition.

Ibn Taymiyya's third argument targets the epistemological circularity inherent in the logicians' concept of definition. If a definition is meant to be the path to conceptualizing the defined, then one must first know the definition is correct. But to know the definition is correct, one must already have conceptualized the defined—which is what the definition was supposed to provide. This creates a vicious circle that makes definition impossible as a source of new knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim about definition and knowledge
Logicians claim that the definition is the path (tariq) to conceptualizing the defined. Find this claim in the text.
2
State the prerequisite for trusting a definition
To use a definition to gain knowledge, one must first know that the definition is correct. How can one know the definition is correct?
3
Recognize the circular dependency
Knowing the definition's correctness requires prior conceptualization of the defined. But that conceptualization is supposed to come from the definition itself. This is a vicious circle.
4
Articulate the conclusion
Therefore, it is impossible to gain new conceptual knowledge through definition, because the process presupposes what it seeks to provide.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نقد المقام الموجب : وينقسم هذا النقد إلى قسمين - قسم يتمثل في صورة حجج يوجهها ابن تيمية إلى هذا المقام - وقسم ينقد به ابن تيمية عناصر الحد وفي هذا القسم الأخير يظهر ابتكاره ظهوراً تاماً على عكس الأول ، إذ يبدو فيه عمله الإبتكاري ضئيلاً . أما القسم الأول - فيتلخص في الحجج الآتية : الحجة الأولى : إن الحد هو قول الحاد ودعواه ، ولكن يشترط ألا يكون الحد مبرهناً عليه ، فهو إذن لا دليل عليه ولا حجة . فالمخاطب إما أن يعلم المحدود بدون قول الحاد أو بقوله . ففي الحالة الأولى لا يكون قد توصل إلى المحدود بالحد وفي الحالة الثانية لا يكون قول الحد - وهو لا دليل عليه ولا برهان - أفاد العلم بشيء . وعلى التقديرين لا يفيد الحد معرفة المحدود . الحجة الثانية : الحد لا يمنع ولا يقام عليه دليل ، بل يبطل بالنقض والمعارضة هذا ما يقرره المناطقة . وهو دليل على أن الحد لا يفيد التصور ، لأنه إذا كان الحاد لا يثبت صحة الحد وصدقه ، كان من الممتنع أن يعرف المخاطب المحدود بالحد لأن قوله يصبح متردداً أو محتملاً للصدق وللكذب ، فتصور المحدود بالحد لا يمكن بدون صدق الحاد . ولا نستطيع أن نعلم صدق الحاد بمجرد الحد لاشتراط عدم البرهنة عليه . إذن فالحد لا يصل بنا إلى المحدود . ولكن المناطقة « يعظمون أمر هذه الحدود » ويرون أنها هي وحدها التي تصل بهم إلى « الكنه » أو « الماهية » أو « الحقيقة » أو بمعنى أدق هي الغاية من اليقين العقلي . ويقيمون العلم كله على أساس الحد . والحد خبر واحد عن أمر عقلي لا حسي ، يحتمل الصدق والكذب وفي الآن عينه لا يقبلون « خبر الواحد » ويرون أنه لا يصل بنا إلى العلم القطعي ، بينما لدى ناقل هذا الخبر من الأدلة الحسية ما يفيد اليقين . فنقل خبر الواحد هو نوع من نقل الحد بل يمتاز عنه أيضاً كما رأينا . إذ أنه يستند على التصور الحسي ، بينما الحد يستند على التصور العقلي . فتفضيل أحدهما على الآخر يعود إلى مجرد تحكم وتعصب في الوضع . الحجة الثالثة : إذا كان الحد هو الطريق إلى تصور المحدود ، فلا يمكن ذلك إلا بعد أن نعلم صحة الحد ، ولا يمكن معرفة صحة الحد قبل تصور المحدود والمحدود لا يمكن التوصل إليه إلا بالحد ، فامتنعت معرفة صحة الحد . الحجة الرابعة : التوصل إلى الحدود بالصفات الذاتية والعرضية . فان لم يعلم الإنسان أن المحدود موصوف بهذه الصفات ، امتنع التصور . لأن من البديهي أن الإنسان لا يتصور ما لا يعلم ، وإن علم أنه موصوف به لم تكن ثمة حاجة إلى الحد . وفي كلتا الحالتين لا يفيد الحد التصور . إننا إذا قلنا « حيوان ناطق » ولم نعلم أنه حد الإنسان ، وجب علينا أولاً أن نعلم النسبة بين الاثنين - بين الحيوانية والناطقية - فاذا لم نكن قد تصورنا الحيوان الناطق ، كان لا بد أولاً أن نتصوره . . . ثم أن نعرف ثانياً النسبة بينه وبين الإنسان .
Critique of the positive position: This critique is divided into two parts—a part consisting of arguments that Ibn Taymiyya directs against this position, and a part in which Ibn Taymiyya critiques the elements of definition. In this latter part his innovation appears fully, contrary to the first, where his innovative work seems minimal. As for the first part, it is summarized in the following arguments. The first argument: The definition is the statement and claim of the definer, but it is stipulated that the definition not be proven; therefore it has no evidence and no proof. The addressee either knows the defined without the definer's statement or by his statement. In the first case, he has not arrived at the defined through the definition; in the second case, the statement of definition—having no evidence or proof—does not convey knowledge of anything. In both cases, the definition does not give knowledge of the defined. The second argument: The definition is not immune and no proof is established for it; rather it is invalidated by counterexample and objection. This is what the logicians affirm. This is evidence that the definition does not produce conceptualization, because if the definer does not establish the correctness and truth of the definition, it is impossible for the addressee to know the defined through the definition, since his statement becomes dubious or subject to truth and falsehood. So conceptualization of the defined by the definition cannot occur without the truth of the definer. And we cannot know the truth of the definer merely by the definition due to the condition that it not be proven. Therefore the definition does not lead us to the defined. But the logicians 'magnify the matter of these definitions' and see that they alone lead to 'the essence' or 'the quiddity' or 'the reality'; or more precisely, they are the goal of intellectual certainty. They build all knowledge on the basis of definition. And the definition is a single report about an intellectual matter, not a sensory one, subject to truth and falsehood. Yet at the same time they do not accept 'the single report' and see that it does not lead us to certain knowledge, while the transmitter of this report has sensory evidence that yields certainty. For transmitting a single report is a type of transmitting a definition, but it also excels over it as we have seen. Because it relies on sensory conceptualization, while the definition relies on intellectual conceptualization. So preferring one over the other amounts to mere willfulness and bias in convention. The third argument: If the definition is the path to conceptualizing the defined, then that is only possible after we know the correctness of the definition; and it is impossible to know the correctness of the definition before conceptualizing the defined—and the defined cannot be reached except by the definition—so knowledge of the correctness of the definition is impossible. The fourth argument: Attaining definitions is through essential and accidental attributes. If a person does not know that the defined is described by these attributes, conceptualization is impossible, because it is self-evident that one cannot conceptualize what one does not know; and if he knows that it is described by them, then there is no need for the definition. In both cases, the definition does not produce conceptualization. If we say 'rational animal' and we do not know that it is the definition of human, we must first know the relation between the two—between animality and rationality—so if we have not conceptualized 'rational animal', we must first conceptualize it... then we must secondarily know the relation between it and human.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage is a critical exposition of Ibn Taymiyya's arguments against the logicians' claim that definitions (hudud) produce conceptual knowledge (tasawwur). The author first divides the critique into two parts: one comprising arguments against the positive position, and another critiquing the elements of definition itself, where Ibn Taymiyya's originality is said to be more apparent. The passage then presents four specific arguments. 

The first argument is based on the nature of definition as an unproven statement. If the listener already knows the defined, the definition is superfluous; if he does not, the definition cannot provide knowledge because it has no proof to establish its truth. 

The second argument points out that logicians themselves admit definitions are not immune to refutation by counterexample or objection. Since the truth of the definition cannot be independently established, the listener cannot be certain that the definition corresponds to reality, and thus it fails to produce certain conceptualization. The author further notes the inconsistency of logicians who accept definitions as certain but reject single-reports (khabar al-wahid) even though the latter often have sensory backing. 

The third argument presents a circularity: to know the definition is correct, one must already conceptualize the defined, but the definition is supposed to be the means to that conceptualization. This creates a vicious circle. 

The fourth argument deals with the attributes used in definitions. If the listener does not know the attributes, he cannot grasp the definition; if he knows them, he already has conceptual knowledge. The example of 'rational animal' as the definition of human illustrates that one would need prior understanding of the relationship between the parts and the whole. 

All four arguments collectively aim to show that definitions, as conceived by logicians, are incapable of yielding new conceptual knowledge. The commentary should note that Ibn Taymiyya's critique is epistemological, not merely logical, and it challenges the foundational role of definitions in the Aristotelian tradition.

التصور
صحة الحد

What is the core logical problem Ibn Taymiyya identifies in the third argument?
A) Definitions are too long to be useful.
B) Definitions require prior knowledge of the defined, which they are supposed to provide, creating a vicious circle.
C) Definitions are always false.
D) Definitions rely on sensory evidence.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya's third argument shows that definitions cannot be the source of conceptual knowledge because they presuppose what they claim to provide.

Ibn Taymiyya's Fourth Argument: The Problem of Attributes
Fourth argument: attributes in definition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's fourth argument that definitions rely on attributes whose knowledge either makes the definition redundant or impossible, and evaluate its logical force.

Core Idea
If you already know the attributes in a definition, you don't need the definition; if you don't, you cannot understand it.

Ibn Taymiyya's final argument in this section focuses on the attributes (essential and accidental) used in definitions. Definitions are built from attributes like 'rational' and 'animal' in the definition of human. To understand a definition, one must already be familiar with these attributes and their relation to the defined. But that familiarity is precisely the conceptual knowledge the definition was supposed to produce. Thus, definitions are either redundant or inaccessible.

Guided Path
1
Understand how definitions are composed
Definitions use essential and accidental attributes (al-ṣifāt al-dhātiyya wa al-ʿaraḍiyya) to describe the defined. For example, 'rational animal' for human.
2
Consider the first scenario: ignorance of attributes
If a person does not know that the defined is described by those attributes, they cannot conceptualize the defined through the definition. Why? Because the definition relies on those attributes.
3
Consider the second scenario: knowledge of attributes
If a person already knows that the defined is described by those attributes, then they already have conceptual knowledge of the defined, making the definition redundant.
4
Apply to the example of 'rational animal'
Ibn Taymiyya uses the example of 'rational animal' as the definition of human. If you don't know what 'rational animal' is, you cannot understand the definition; if you do, you already know what human is.

Critique of Logicians' Overvaluation of Definitions
Critique of logicians' overvaluation of definitions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the critique of logicians' overreliance on definitions and identify the epistemological inconsistency between their treatment of hadd and khabar al-wahid.

Core Idea
Definitions are rational reports; khabar al-wahid is sensory-based. Logicians' preference is arbitrary.

This passage challenges the foundational role of definitions in logic. The author argues that definitions, being rational reports that can be true or false, are not inherently certain. In contrast, transmitted reports (khabar al-wahid) rely on sensory evidence and can yield certainty. Yet logicians dismiss the latter while exalting the former. This lesson unpacks that critique and its implications for the epistemology of Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' claim about definitions
Find where the author states that logicians 'exaggerate' definitions and consider them the path to essence and certainty. Underline the phrases that show their high estimation.
2
Extract the author's characterization of definition
Notice that the author defines hadd as a 'single report about a rational, not sensory, matter.' This makes it a kind of khabar. Highlight this in the Arabic.
3
Compare with khabar al-wahid
The author points out that khabar al-wahid is also a report, but it is based on sensory evidence. Identify the sentence that contrasts sensory vs. rational conceptualization.
4
Analyze the inconsistency
Logicians reject khabar al-wahid as non-certain, yet accept definitions which are equally reports. Why does the author call this arbitrary? Look for the phrase 'mere arbitrary and biased'.
5
Conclude the author's position
The author suggests that transmitted reports are a type of definition and are actually more reliable because they rest on sensory evidence. Summarize his main point.

صدق الحاد بمجرد الحد لاشتراط عدم البرهنة عليه . إذن فالحد لا يصل بنا إلى المحدود . ولكن المناطقة « يعظمون أمر هذه الحدود » ويرون أنها هي وحدها التي تصل بهم إلى « الكنه » أو « الماهية » أو « الحقيقة » أو بمعنى أدق هي الغاية من اليقين العقلي . ويقيمون العلم كله على أساس الحد . والحد خبر واحد عن أمر عقلي لا حسي ، يحتمل الصدق والكذب وفي الآن عينه لا يقبلون « خبر الواحد » ويرون أنه لا يصل بنا إلى العلم القطعي ، بينما لدى ناقل هذا الخبر من الأدلة الحسية ما يفيد اليقين . فنقل خبر الواحد هو نوع من نقل الحد بل يمتاز عنه أيضاً كما رأينا . إذ أنه يستند على التصور الحسي ، بينما الحد يستند على التصور العقلي . فتفضيل أحدهما على الآخر يعود إلى مجرد تحكم وتعصب في الوضع .
The truthfulness of the [؟] is by mere definition, due to the condition of not demonstrating it. So the definition does not lead us to the defined. But the logicians 'exaggerate the matter of these definitions' and consider that they alone lead them to the 'essence' or the 'quiddity' or the 'reality', or more precisely, they are the goal of rational certainty. And they build all knowledge on the basis of definition. And definition is a single report about a rational, not sensory, matter, which can be true or false, and at the same time they do not accept 'the report of a single person' and consider that it does not lead us to certain knowledge, whereas the transmitter of this report has sensory evidence that provides certainty. So the transmission of a single report is a type of transmission of definition, but it is also distinguished from it, as we saw, because it relies on sensory conceptualization, while definition relies on rational conceptualization. So preferring one over the other is merely arbitrary and biased in position.
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage is a critical analysis of the logicians' heavy reliance on definitions (hadd). The author argues that definitions are merely rational reports (khabar) that can be true or false, yet logicians elevate them as the sole path to certainty, dismissing transmitted reports (khabar al-wahid) which are based on sensory evidence. The author points out the inconsistency: if definitions are uncertain, and sensory reports are certain, then preferring definitions is arbitrary. The example of transmitted reports from hadith sciences (khabar al-wahid) is used to highlight that sensory-based knowledge can yield certainty, unlike rational definitions. The critique exposes a bias in logicians' methodology.

Key Terms
الحد
المحدود
خبر الواحد
اليقين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the author claim that logicians' preference for definition over transmitted reports (khabar al-wahid) is arbitrary?
A) Because definitions are sensory while khabar al-wahid is rational.
B) Because definitions are certain while khabar al-wahid is not.
C) Because both are reports but definitions are less reliable than khabar al-wahid which is based on sensory evidence.
D) Because definitions are derived from scripture while khabar al-wahid is not.

Key Takeaway
The author argues that definitions are not inherently more certain than transmitted reports, and logicians' preference is a matter of bias rather than epistemology.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task 1: Compare the epistemological status of hadd and khabar al-wahid as presented in the passage. Then write a brief argument defending the logicians' position using the tools from earlier lessons (e.g., universals).
?
Task 2: Find a real example from fiqh where a ruling is established by a khabar al-wahid (e.g., a solitary hadith on a specific issue) and evaluate whether its certainty is stronger or weaker than a definition-based rational argument.

The Circularity Argument Against Definitions
Presents a circularity argument against the utility of definitions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the circularity (dawr) argument against definitions and evaluate its logical force.

Core Idea
If definitions require prior knowledge of the defined, they can never be verified.

This lesson examines a powerful objection to the foundational role of definitions in logic. The argument claims that we cannot know a definition is correct without already having the concept it is supposed to provide. This creates a vicious circle, potentially undermining the whole enterprise of definition-based conceptualization.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional premise
Find the 'if...then' structure in the Arabic. The argument states: If definition is the path to conceptualization, then we must first know the definition is correct to use it.
The conditional clause is: 'إذا كان الحد هو الطريق إلى تصور المحدود'.
2
Trace the dependency chain
Follow the sequence: Knowledge of correctness (صحة الحد) requires prior conceptualization of the defined (تصور المحدود). But the defined can only be reached through definition (الحد). So correctness depends on definition, and definition depends on correctness.
3
Locate the vicious circle
The circle emerges: to confirm the definition is correct, you need the concept; to get the concept, you need the correct definition. Neither can be established first.
This is a classic example of circular reasoning (دور) in epistemology of definitions.
4
Evaluate whether the circle is genuinely vicious
Consider if the circle can be broken. For instance, if conceptualization is possible through other means (e.g., direct experience, innate knowledge, or ostensive definition), the argument loses force. Assess the strength of the premise that definition is the only path.
Many Islamic scholars argued that simple concepts (like existence) cannot be defined; they are known intuitively.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : إذا كان الحد هو الطريق إلى تصور المحدود ، فلا يمكن ذلك إلا بعد أن نعلم صحة الحد ، ولا يمكن معرفة صحة الحد قبل تصور المحدود ـ والمحدود لا يمكن التوصل إليه إلا بالحد ، فامتنعت معرفة صحة الحد .
The third argument: If definition is the path to conceptualizing the defined, then that is only possible after we know the correctness of the definition, and it is not possible to know the correctness of the definition before conceptualizing the defined — and the defined can only be reached through definition, so knowledge of the correctness of the definition is impossible.
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage presents a classic circularity (dawr) argument against definitions. The claim is that if definition is the only way to attain a concept (tasawwur) of the defined, then one must first know that the definition is correct (sahih). But verifying the correctness of a definition requires already conceptualizing the defined, which in turn depends on the definition. This creates a vicious circle, making it impossible to ever confirm the correctness of any definition. The argument targets the foundational role logicians assign to definitions.

Key Terms
الحد
تصور
صحة الحد
الدور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the third argument claim about the relationship between definition and knowledge of its correctness?
A) Definition can be verified independently of the defined.
B) Knowledge of the definition's correctness requires prior conceptualization of the defined, which is only possible through the definition.
C) The defined can be known without the definition.
D) The definition's correctness is self-evident.

Key Takeaway
The circularity argument shows that definitions cannot be the sole means of acquiring concepts, since verification of a definition presupposes the very concept it is supposed to provide.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a similar circularity argument for a definition used in Islamic law, such as the definition of 'riba' (usury) as 'any excess in a loan of a specific kind'. Show how verifying this definition requires prior knowledge of what counts as riba.
?
Evaluate whether the circle can be broken by appealing to scriptural texts (nass) or the consensus of scholars (ijma') as providing the concept independently of the definition.
